<p>If the federal government agrees to discuss an association agreement, the conditions would be negotiated between the two entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-113">[100]</a></sup> The agreement might cover topics such as the role of the U.S. military in Puerto Rico, the use of the US currency, free trade between the two entities, and whether <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans" title="Puerto Ricans">Puerto Ricans</a> would be U.S. citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The three current Free Associated States (<a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshall Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Micronesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>) use the American dollar, receive some financial support and the promise of military defense if they refuse military access to any other country. Their citizens are allowed to work in the U.S. and serve in its military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-113">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Governor <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Rossell%C3%B3" title="Ricardo Rosselló">Ricardo Rosselló</a> is strongly in favor of statehood to help develop the economy and help to "solve our 500-year-old colonial dilemma ... Colonialism is not an option .... It’s a civil rights issue ... 3.5 million citizens seeking an absolute democracy," he told the news media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[102]</a></sup> Benefits of statehood include an additional $10 billion per year in federal funds, the right to vote in presidential elections, higher Social Security and Medicare benefits, and a right for its government agencies and municipalities to file for bankruptcy. The latter is currently prohibited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danica_Coto_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danica_Coto-116">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Statehood might be useful as a means of dealing with the financial crisis, since it would allow for bankruptcy and the relevant protection. According to the Government Development Bank, this might be the only solution to the debt crisis. Congress has the power to vote to allow Chapter 9 protection without the need for statehood, but in late 2015 there was very little support in the House for this concept. Other benefits to statehood include increased disability benefits and Medicaid funding, the right to vote in Presidential elections and the higher (federal) minimum wage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Subsequent to the 2017 referendum, Puerto Rico's legislators are also expected to vote on a bill that would allow the Governor to draft a state constitution and hold elections to choose senators and representatives to the federal Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[106]</a></sup> In spite of the outcome of the referendum, and the so-called Tennessee Plan (above), action by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> would be necessary to implement changes to the status of Puerto Rico under the <a href="/wiki/Article_Four_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Federal_property_and_the_Territorial_Clause" title="Article Four of the United States Constitution">Territorial Clause of the United States Constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danica_Coto_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Danica_Coto-116">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations_Special_Committee_on_Decolonization">United Nations Special Committee on Decolonization</span></h3>
<p>Since 1953, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> has been considering the <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Political status of Puerto Rico">political status of Puerto Rico</a> and how to assist it in achieving "independence" or "decolonization". In 1978, the Special Committee determined that a "colonial relationship" existed between the US and Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-colonial_relationship_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-colonial_relationship-120">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UN's <a href="/wiki/Special_Committee_on_Decolonization" title="Special Committee on Decolonization">Special Committee on Decolonization</a> has often referred to Puerto Rico as a "nation" in its reports, because, internationally, the people of Puerto Rico are often considered to be a Caribbean nation with their own national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[110]</a></sup> Most recently, in a June 2016 report, the Special Committee called for the United States to expedite the process to allow self-determination in Puerto Rico. More specifically, the group called on the United States to expedite a process that would allow the people of Puerto Rico to exercise fully their right to self-determination and independence. ... allow the Puerto Rican people to take decisions in a sovereign manner, and to address their urgent economic and social needs, including unemployment, marginalization, insolvency and poverty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Geography of Puerto Rico">Geography of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Geology of Puerto Rico">Geology of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rico_(1).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="321" data-file-width="631" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Rico_%281%29.png/550px-Rico_%281%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/Rico_%281%29.png 1.5x" width="550"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rico_(1).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of Puerto Rico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Puerto Rico consists of the main island of Puerto Rico and various smaller islands, including <a href="/wiki/Vieques,_Puerto_Rico" title="Vieques, Puerto Rico">Vieques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culebra,_Puerto_Rico" title="Culebra, Puerto Rico">Culebra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mona,_Puerto_Rico" title="Mona, Puerto Rico">Mona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desecheo_Island" title="Desecheo Island">Desecheo</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caja_de_Muertos,_Puerto_Rico" title="Caja de Muertos, Puerto Rico">Caja de Muertos</a>. Of these five, only Culebra and Vieques are inhabited year-round. Culebra, which is merely 29 km (18 miles) away from the mainland, is home to <a href="/wiki/Flamenco_Beach" title="Flamenco Beach">Flamenco Beach</a>, considered by some travel writers as one of the top ten beaches in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn.com_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn.com-125">[112]</a></sup> Mona, which has played a key role in maritime history, is uninhabited most of the year except for employees of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Natural_and_Environmental_Resources_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Department of Natural and Environmental Resources (Puerto Rico)">Puerto Rico Department of Natural Resources</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[113]</a></sup> There are many other even smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islets" title="Islets">islets</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Monito_Island" title="Monito Island">Monito</a>, which is near to Mona,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[114]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isla_de_Cabras" title="Isla de Cabras">Isla de Cabras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isleta_de_San_Juan" title="Isleta de San Juan">La Isleta de San Juan</a>, both located on the <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Bay" title="San Juan Bay">San Juan Bay</a>. The latter is the only inhabited islet with communities like <a href="/wiki/Old_San_Juan" title="Old San Juan">Old San Juan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerta_de_Tierra" title="Puerta de Tierra">Puerta de Tierra</a>, and connected to the main island by bridges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Commonwealth of Puerto Rico has an area of 13,790 square kilometers (5,320 sq mi), of which 8,870 km<sup>2</sup> (3,420 sq mi) is land and 4,921 km<sup>2</sup> (1,900 sq mi) is water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciafactbook_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-ciafactbook-130">[117]</a></sup> Puerto Rico is larger than two U.S. states, Delaware and Rhode Island. The maximum length of the main island from east to west is 180 km (110 mi), and the maximum width from north to south is 65 km (40 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-topuertorico.com_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-topuertorico.com-131">[118]</a></sup> Puerto Rico is the smallest of the Greater Antilles. It is 80% of the size of <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[119]</a></sup> just over 18% of the size of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> and 8% of the size of Cuba, the largest of the Greater Antilles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The island is mostly mountainous with large coastal areas in the north and south. The main mountain range is called "<a href="/wiki/Cordillera_Central_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Cordillera Central (Puerto Rico)">La Cordillera Central</a>" (The Central Range). The highest elevation in Puerto Rico, <a href="/wiki/Cerro_de_Punta" title="Cerro de Punta">Cerro de Punta</a> 1,338 meters (4,390 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciafactbook_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-ciafactbook-130">[117]</a></sup> is located in this range.</p>
<p>Another important peak is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Yunque,_Puerto_Rico" title="El Yunque, Puerto Rico">El Yunque</a>, one of the highest in the <i>Sierra de Luquillo</i> at the <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_National_Forest" title="El Yunque National Forest">El Yunque National Forest</a>, with an elevation of 1,065 m (3,494 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[121]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg/300px-Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg/450px-Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg/600px-Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patillas_Puerto_Rico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The coast at Patillas, Puerto Rico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Puerto Rico has 17 lakes, all man-made, and more than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Puerto_Rico_rivers" title="List of Puerto Rico rivers">50 rivers</a>, most originating in the Cordillera Central.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[122]</a></sup> Rivers in the northern region of the island are typically longer and of higher <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_flow_rate" title="Volumetric flow rate">water flow rates</a> than those of the south, since the south receives less rain than the central and northern regions.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico is composed of <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> to <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic" title="Volcanic">volcanic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonic" title="Plutonic">plutonic</a> rocks, overlain by younger <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> and more recent <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonates</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rocks" title="Sedimentary rocks">sedimentary rocks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[123]</a></sup> Most of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caverns" title="Caverns">caverns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karst</a> topography on the island occurs in the northern region in the carbonates. The oldest rocks are approximately <span class="nowrap">190 million</span> years old (<a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a>) and are located at Sierra Bermeja in the southwest part of the island. They may represent part of the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> and are believed to come from the Pacific Ocean realm.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico lies at the boundary between the Caribbean and North American <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plates</a> and is being deformed by the <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> stresses caused by their interaction. These stresses may cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthquakes" title="Earthquakes">earthquakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>. These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic" title="Seismic">seismic</a> events, along with <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, represent some of the most dangerous <a href="/wiki/Geologic_hazards" title="Geologic hazards">geologic hazards</a> in the island and in the northeastern Caribbean.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1918_San_Ferm%C3%ADn_earthquake" title="1918 San Fermín earthquake">most recent major earthquake</a> occurred on <span class="nowrap">October 11</span>, 1918, and had an estimated magnitude of 7.5 on the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1918_quake_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-1918_quake-137">[124]</a></sup> It originated off the coast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aguadilla" title="Aguadilla">Aguadilla</a>, several kilometers off the northern coast, and was accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>. It caused extensive property damage and widespread losses, damaging infrastructure, especially bridges. It resulted in an estimated 116 deaths and $4 million in property damage. The failure of the government to move rapidly to provide for the general welfare contributed to political activism by opponents and eventually to the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Nationalist_Party" title="Puerto Rican Nationalist Party">Puerto Rican Nationalist Party</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Trench" title="Puerto Rico Trench">Puerto Rico Trench</a>, the largest and deepest trench in the Atlantic, is located about 115 km (71 mi) north of Puerto Rico at the boundary between the Caribbean and North American plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trench_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-trench-138">[125]</a></sup> It is 280 km (170 mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[126]</a></sup> At its deepest point, named the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Deep" title="Milwaukee Deep">Milwaukee Deep</a>, it is almost 8,400 m (27,600 ft) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trench_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-trench-138">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Climate of Puerto Rico">Climate of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The climate of Puerto Rico in the Köppen climate classification is <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a>. Temperatures are warm to hot year round, averaging near 85 °F (29 °C) in lower elevations and 70 °F (21 °C) in the mountains. Easterly trade winds pass across the island year round. Puerto Rico has a rainy season which stretches from April into November. The mountains of the Cordillera Central are the main cause of the variations in the temperature and rainfall that occur over very short distances. The mountains can also cause wide variation in local wind speed and direction due to their sheltering and channeling effects adding to the climatic variation.</p>
<p>The island has an average temperature of 82.4 °F (28 °C) throughout the year, with an average minimum temperature of 66.9 °F (19 °C) and maximum of 85.4 °F (30 °C). Daily temperature changes seasonally are quite small in the lowlands and coastal areas. The temperature in the south is usually a few degrees higher than the north and temperatures in the central interior mountains are always cooler than those on the rest of the island.</p>
<p>Between the dry and wet season, there is a temperature change of around 6 °F (3.3 °C). This is mainly due to the warm waters of the tropical <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, which significantly modify cooler air moving in from the north and northwest. Coastal waters temperatures around the years are about 75 °F (24 °C) in February to 85 °F (29 °C) in August. The highest temperature ever recorded was 99 °F (37 °C) at <a href="/wiki/Arecibo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Arecibo, Puerto Rico">Arecibo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[127]</a></sup> while the lowest temperature ever recorded was 40 °F (4 °C) in the mountains at <a href="/wiki/Adjuntas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Adjuntas, Puerto Rico">Adjuntas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aibonito,_Puerto_Rico" title="Aibonito, Puerto Rico">Aibonito</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corozal,_Puerto_Rico" title="Corozal, Puerto Rico">Corozal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA-PR_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-PR-141">[128]</a></sup> The average yearly precipitation is 1,687 mm (66 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daly2003_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daly2003-142">[129]</a></sup></p>

<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.weather.com/weather/wxclimatology/monthly/graph/USPR0087" rel="nofollow">"Average Weather for San Juan, PR"</a>. <a href="/wiki/The_Weather_Channel" title="The Weather Channel">The Weather Channel</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">March 22,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APuerto+Rico&amp;rft.btitle=Average+Weather+for+San+Juan%2C+PR&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=The+Weather+Channel&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.weather.com%2Fweather%2Fwxclimatology%2Fmonthly%2Fgraph%2FUSPR0087&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>Puerto Rico experiences the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="Atlantic hurricane season">Atlantic hurricane season</a>, similar to the remainder of the Caribbean Sea and North Atlantic oceans. On average, a quarter of its annual rainfall is contributed from tropical cyclones, which are more prevalent during periods of La Niña than <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[130]</a></sup> A cyclone of tropical storm strength passes near Puerto Rico, on average, every five years. A hurricane passes in the vicinity of the island, on average, every seven years. Since 1851, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1928_Okeechobee_Hurricane" title="1928 Okeechobee Hurricane">Lake Okeechobee Hurricane</a> of September 1928 is the only hurricane to make landfall as a Category 5 hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the busy <a href="/wiki/2017_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2017 Atlantic hurricane season">2017 Atlantic hurricane season</a>, Puerto Rico avoided a direct hit by the Category 5 <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Irma" title="Hurricane Irma">Hurricane Irma</a> on September 8, 2017, but high winds caused a loss of electrical power to some one million residents. Almost 50% of hospitals were operating with power provided by generators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[132]</a></sup> The Category 4 <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Jose_(2017)" title="Hurricane Jose (2017)">Hurricane Jose</a>, as expected, veered away from Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[133]</a></sup> A short time later, the devastating <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a> made landfall in Puerto Rico as a Category 4 hurricane, with sustained 155-MPH winds, powerful rains and widespread flooding causing tremendous destruction, including the electrical grid, which could remain out for 4-6 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MariaDisc18_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-MariaDisc18-147">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[136]</a></sup> With such widespread destruction and a great need for supplies - everything from drinking water, food, medicine and personal care items to fuel for generators and construction materials for rebuilding the island - Gov. Rossello and several Congressmen called on the federal government to waive the WWI-era Jones Act (protectionist provisions: ships made and owned in U.S., and with U.S. crews), which essentially double Puerto Rico's cost for shipped goods relative to neighboring islands. On September 28, US President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> waived the Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Fauna of Puerto Rico">Fauna of Puerto Rico</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flora_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Flora of Puerto Rico">Flora of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg"><img alt='"In this image there is a brown coquí. The species resembles a small frog."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="300" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg/150px-Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg/225px-Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Common_Coqu%C3%AD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Common Coquí</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Species <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_(ecology)" title="Endemic (ecology)">endemic</a> to the archipelago number 239 plants, 16 birds and 39 <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>/<a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, recognized as of 1998. Most of these (234, 12 and 33 respectively) are found on the main island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[138]</a></sup> The most recognizable endemic species and a symbol of Puerto Rican pride is the <i><a href="/wiki/Coqu%C3%AD" title="Coquí">coquí</a></i>, a small frog easily identified by the sound of its call, from which it gets its name. Most <i>coquí</i> species (13 of 17) live in the <a href="/wiki/El_Yunque_National_Forest" title="El Yunque National Forest">El Yunque National Forest</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">tropical rainforest</a> in the northeast of the island previously known as the Caribbean National Forest. El Yunque is home to more than 240 plants, 26 of which are endemic to the island. It is also home to 50 bird species, including the critically endangered <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_amazon" title="Puerto Rican amazon">Puerto Rican amazon</a>.</p>
<p>Across the island in the southwest, the 40 km<sup>2</sup> (15 sq mi) of dry land at the Guánica Commonwealth Forest Reserve<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[139]</a></sup> contain over 600 uncommon species of plants and animals, including 48 endangered species and 16 endemic to Puerto Rico.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Government of Puerto Rico">Government of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Politics of Puerto Rico">Politics of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>Puerto Rico has <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_senatorial_districts" title="Puerto Rico senatorial districts">8 senatorial districts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_representative_districts" title="Puerto Rico representative districts">40 representative districts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Municipalities of Puerto Rico">78 municipalities</a>. It has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_republic" title="Constitutional republic">republican form of government</a> with <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> subject to the jurisdiction and sovereignty of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-153">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ussd_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-ussd-154">[141]</a></sup> Its current powers are all delegated by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> and lack full protection under the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Department_of_State_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Department_of_State-155">[142]</a></sup> Puerto Rico's head of state is the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>.</p>
<p>The government of Puerto Rico, based on <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">the formal republican system</a>, is composed of three branches: the <a href="/wiki/Executive_branch_of_the_government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Executive branch of the government of Puerto Rico">executive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legislative_branch_of_the_government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Legislative branch of the government of Puerto Rico">legislative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Judiciary of Puerto Rico">judicial branch</a>. The executive branch is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Governor of Puerto Rico">governor</a>, currently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ricky_Rossell%C3%B3" title="Ricky Rosselló">Ricky Rosselló</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_branch" title="Legislative branch">legislative branch</a> consists of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> legislature called the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico">Legislative Assembly</a>, made up of a <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Senate of Puerto Rico">Senate</a> as its upper chamber and a <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_of_Puerto_Rico" title="House of Representatives of Puerto Rico">House of Representatives</a> as its lower chamber. The Senate is headed by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Senate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="President of the Senate of Puerto Rico">President of the Senate</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Rivera_Schatz" title="Thomas Rivera Schatz">Thomas Rivera Schatz</a>, while the House of Representatives is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_House_of_Representatives_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Speaker of the House of Representatives of Puerto Rico">Speaker of the House</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Johnny_M%C3%A9ndez" title="Carlos Johnny Méndez">Johnny Méndez</a>. The governor and legislators are elected by popular vote every four years with <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_general_election,_2016" title="Puerto Rican general election, 2016">the last election held in November 2016</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="839" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG/290px-First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG/435px-First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG/580px-First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_Supreme_Court_PR.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The First Supreme Court of Puerto Rico, appointed pursuant to the <a href="/wiki/Foraker_Act" title="Foraker Act">Foraker Act</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Judiciary of Puerto Rico">judicial branch</a> is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Chief Justice of the Supreme Court of Puerto Rico">Chief Justice of the Supreme Court of Puerto Rico</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Maite_Oronoz_Rodr%C3%ADguez" title="Maite Oronoz Rodríguez">Maite Oronoz Rodríguez</a>. Members of the judicial branch are appointed by the governor with the <a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent" title="Advice and consent">advice and consent</a> of the Senate.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico is represented in the United States Congress by a nonvoting delegate, the <a href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Resident Commissioner of Puerto Rico">Resident Commissioner</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Jenniffer_Gonz%C3%A1lez" title="Jenniffer González">Jenniffer González</a>. Current congressional rules have removed the Commissioner's power to vote in the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_Whole_(United_States_House_of_Representatives)" title="Committee of the Whole (United States House of Representatives)">Committee of the Whole</a>, but the Commissioner can vote in committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rhg_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-rhg-156">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Puerto Rican elections are governed by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission" title="Federal Election Commission">Federal Election Commission</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Elections_Commission_of_Puerto_Rico" title="State Elections Commission of Puerto Rico">State Elections Commission of Puerto Rico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[144]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2013)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[145]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (February 2013)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> While residing in Puerto Rico, Puerto Ricans cannot vote in U.S. presidential elections, but they can vote in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Presidential_Primary" title="US Presidential Primary">primaries</a>. Puerto Ricans who become residents of a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a> can vote in presidential elections.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico hosts <a href="/wiki/Consul_(representative)" title="Consul (representative)">consulates</a> from 41 countries, mainly from the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and Europe, with most located in San Juan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[146]</a></sup> As an unincorporated territory of the United States, Puerto Rico does not have any first-order administrative divisions as defined by the U.S. government, but has 78 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipalities" title="Municipalities">municipalities</a> at the second level. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mona_Island" title="Mona Island">Mona Island</a> is not a municipality, but part of the municipality of <a href="/wiki/Mayag%C3%BCez,_Puerto_Rico" title="Mayagüez, Puerto Rico">Mayagüez</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Municipalities are subdivided into wards or <a href="/wiki/Barrio" title="Barrio">barrios</a>, and those into sectors. Each municipality has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayors_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Mayors of Puerto Rico">mayor</a> and a municipal legislature elected for a four-year term. The municipality of <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a> (previously called "town"), was founded first, in 1521, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Germ%C3%A1n" title="San Germán">San Germán</a> in 1570, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coamo" title="Coamo">Coamo</a> in 1579, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arecibo" title="Arecibo">Arecibo</a> in 1614, <a href="/wiki/Aguada,_Puerto_Rico" title="Aguada, Puerto Rico">Aguada</a> in 1692 and <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce</a> in 1692. An increase of settlement saw the founding of 30 municipalities in the 18th century and 34 in the 19th. Six were founded in the 20th century; the last was <a href="/wiki/Florida,_Puerto_Rico" title="Florida, Puerto Rico">Florida</a> in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fundacion_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-fundacion-161">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">Political culture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Political party strength in Puerto Rico">Political party strength in Puerto Rico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2445" data-file-width="2445" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png/220px-Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png/330px-Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png/440px-Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Political-party-strength-in-puerto-rico-2012.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The difference between the incumbent party, the PPD, and its opponent, the PNP, was a mere 0.6% in the last election. This difference is common as the political landscape experiences political cycles between both parties, with the PPD ruling all branches of government for 36 of the past 64 years. The PNP, on the other hand, has ruled both the executive and legislative branch concurrently for 16 years. The other 12 years experienced a <a href="/wiki/Divided_government_in_the_United_States" title="Divided government in the United States">divided government</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1952, Puerto Rico has had three main political parties: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_Democratic_Party_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Popular Democratic Party of Puerto Rico">Popular Democratic Party</a> (PPD in Spanish), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Progressive_Party_of_Puerto_Rico" title="New Progressive Party of Puerto Rico">New Progressive Party</a> (PNP in Spanish) and the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Independence_Party" title="Puerto Rican Independence Party">Puerto Rican Independence Party</a> (PIP). The three parties stand for different political status. The PPD, for example, seeks <a href="/wiki/Status_quo_movement_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Status quo movement in Puerto Rico">to maintain the island's status with the U.S. as a commonwealth</a>, while the PNP, on the other hand, seeks <a href="/wiki/Statehood_movement_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Statehood movement in Puerto Rico">to make Puerto Rico a state of the United States</a>. The PIP, in contrast, seeks a complete separation from the United States by seeking <a href="/wiki/Independence_movement_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Independence movement in Puerto Rico">to make Puerto Rico a sovereign nation</a>. In terms of party strength, the PPD and PNP usually hold about 47% of the vote each while the PIP holds only about 5%.</p>
<p>After 2007, other parties emerged on the island. The first, the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans_for_Puerto_Rico_Party" title="Puerto Ricans for Puerto Rico Party">Puerto Ricans for Puerto Rico Party</a> (PPR in Spanish) was registered that same year. The party claims that it seeks to address the islands' problems from a status-neutral platform. But it ceased to remain as a registered party when it failed to obtain the required number of votes in the 2008 general election. Four years later, the 2012 election saw the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Movimiento_Uni%C3%B3n_Soberanista" title="Movimiento Unión Soberanista">Movimiento Unión Soberanista</a> (MUS; English: <i>Sovereign Union Movement</i>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partido_del_Pueblo_Trabajador" title="Partido del Pueblo Trabajador">Partido del Pueblo Trabajador</a> (PPT; English: <i>Working People's Party</i>) but none obtained more than 1% of the vote.</p>
<p>Other non-registered parties include the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Nationalist_Party" title="Puerto Rican Nationalist Party">Puerto Rican Nationalist Party</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_Workers_Movement_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Socialist Workers Movement (Puerto Rico)">Socialist Workers Movement</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hostosian_National_Independence_Movement" title="Hostosian National Independence Movement">Hostosian National Independence Movement</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Constitution of Puerto Rico">Constitution of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Law of Puerto Rico">Law of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The insular legal system is a blend of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> systems.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico is the only current U.S. possession whose legal system operates primarily in a language other than American English: namely, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>. Because the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. federal government</a> operates primarily in English, all Puerto Rican attorneys must be bilingual in order to litigate in English in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_federal_courts" title="United States federal courts">U.S. federal courts</a>, and litigate federal preemption issues in Puerto Rican courts.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Title_48_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 48 of the United States Code">Title 48 of the United States Code</a> outlines the role of the United States Code to United States territories and insular areas such as Puerto Rico. After the U.S. government assumed control of Puerto Rico in 1901, it initiated legal reforms resulting in the adoption of codes of criminal law, criminal procedure, and civil procedure modeled after those then in effect in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. Although Puerto Rico has since followed the federal example of transferring criminal and civil procedure from statutory law to rules promulgated by the judiciary, several portions of its criminal law still reflect the influence of the <a href="/wiki/California_Penal_Code" title="California Penal Code">California Penal Code</a>.</p>
<p>The judicial branch is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice" title="Chief Justice">Chief Justice</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Supreme_Court" title="Puerto Rico Supreme Court">Puerto Rico Supreme Court</a>, which is the only appellate court required by the Constitution. All other courts are created by the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico">Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico</a>. There is also a <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_District_of_Puerto_Rico" title="United States District Court for the District of Puerto Rico">Federal District Court for Puerto Rico</a>. Someone accused of a criminal act at the federal level may not be accused for the same act in a Commonwealth court, unlike a state court, since Puerto Rico as a territory lacks sovereignty separate from Congress as a state does.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mart.C3.ADnez_Torres_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mart.C3.ADnez_Torres-162">[149]</a></sup> Such a parallel accusation would constitute <a href="/wiki/Double_jeopardy" title="Double jeopardy">double jeopardy</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_government">Law and government</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">Crime</span></h3>
<p>The overall rate of crime is low in Puerto Rico. The territory has a high firearm homicide rate. The homicide rate of 19.2 per 100,000 inhabitants was significantly higher than any U.S. state in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[151]</a></sup> Most homicide victims are gang members and drug traffickers with about 80% of homicides in Puerto Rico being drug related.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_status">Political status</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Political status of Puerto Rico">Political status of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proposed_political_status_for_Puerto_Rico" title="Proposed political status for Puerto Rico">Proposed political status for Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The nature of Puerto Rico's political relationship with the U.S. is the subject of ongoing debate in Puerto Rico, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdc_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdc-166">[153]</a></sup> Specifically, the basic question is whether Puerto Rico should remain a <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">U.S. territory</a>, become a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a>, or become an independent country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Estado_Libre_Asociado"><i>Estado Libre Asociado</i></span></h4>
<p>In 1950, the U.S. Congress granted Puerto Ricans the right to organize a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_meeting)" title="Constitutional convention (political meeting)">constitutional convention</a> via a referendum that gave them the option of voting their preference, "yes" or "no", on a proposed U.S. law that would organize Puerto Rico as a "commonwealth" that would continue United States sovereignty over Puerto Rico and its people. Puerto Rico's electorate expressed its support for this measure in 1951 with a second referendum to ratify the constitution. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Constitution of Puerto Rico">Constitution of Puerto Rico</a> was formally adopted on July 3, 1952. The Constitutional Convention specified the name by which the <a href="/wiki/Body_politic" title="Body politic">body politic</a> would be known.</p>
<p>On February 4, 1952, the convention approved Resolution 22 which chose in English the word <i><a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_(U.S._insular_area)" title="Commonwealth (U.S. insular area)">Commonwealth</a></i>, meaning a "politically organized community" or "state", which is simultaneously connected by a compact or treaty to another political system. Puerto Rico officially designates itself with the term "Commonwealth of Puerto Rico" in its constitution, as a translation into English of the term to "Estado Libre Asociado" (ELA).</p>
<p>In 1967 Puerto Rico's Legislative Assembly polled the political preferences of the Puerto Rican electorate by passing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebiscite" title="Plebiscite">plebiscite</a> act that provided for a vote on the status of Puerto Rico. This constituted the first plebiscite by the Legislature for a choice among three status options (commonwealth, statehood, and independence). In subsequent plebiscites organized by Puerto Rico held in 1993 and 1998 (without any formal commitment on the part of the U.S. Government to honor the results), the current political status failed to receive majority support. In 1993, Commonwealth status won by a plurality of votes (48.6% versus 46.3% for statehood), while the "none of the above" option, which was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_Democratic_Party_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Popular Democratic Party of Puerto Rico">Popular Democratic Party</a>-sponsored choice, won in 1998 with 50.3% of the votes (versus 46.5% for statehood). Disputes arose as to the definition of each of the ballot alternatives, and Commonwealth advocates, among others, reportedly urged a vote for "none of the above".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[157]</a></sup> The latest referendum on statehood, independence, or an associated republic was held on November 6, 2012. The people of Puerto Rico made history by requesting, for the first time ever, the conclusion of the island's current territorial status. Almost 78% of registered voters participated in a plebiscite held to resolve Puerto Rico's status, and a clear majority (54%) disagreed with Puerto Rico maintaining its present territorial status. Furthermore, among the possible alternatives, sixty-one percent (61%) of voters chose the statehood option, while one third of the ballots were submitted blank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puerto_Ricans_favor_statehood_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puerto_Ricans_favor_statehood-171">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Puerto_Ricans_opt_for_statehood_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Puerto_Ricans_opt_for_statehood-172">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 11, 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico">Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico</a> enacted a <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_resolution" title="Concurrent resolution">concurrent resolution</a> requesting the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States">Congress of the United States</a> to respond to the referendum of the people of Puerto Rico, held on November 6, 2012, to end its current form of territorial status and to begin the process to admit Puerto Rico as a State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-113">[100]</a></sup> The initiative has not made Puerto Rico into a state.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Within_the_United_States">Within the United States</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PR_Capitol,_south_side.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1672" data-file-width="2124" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG/300px-PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG/450px-PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG/600px-PR_Capitol%2C_south_side.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PR_Capitol,_south_side.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Capitol_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Capitol of Puerto Rico">Capitol of Puerto Rico</a>, home of the Legislative Assembly in Puerto Rico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Constitutionally, Puerto Rico is subject to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plenary_powers" title="Plenary powers">plenary powers</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_clause" title="Territorial clause">territorial clause</a> of <a href="/wiki/Article_Four_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article Four of the United States Constitution">Article IV of the U.S. Constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[160]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._federal_law" title="U.S. federal law">Laws enacted at the federal level in the United States</a> apply to Puerto Rico as well, regardless of its political status. Their residents <a href="/wiki/Federal_voting_rights_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Federal voting rights in Puerto Rico">do not have voting representation in the U.S. Congress</a>. Like the different states of the United States, Puerto Rico lacks "the full sovereignty of an independent nation", for example, the power to manage its "external relations with other nations", which is held by the U.S. federal government. The Supreme Court of the United States has indicated that once the U.S. Constitution has been extended to an area (by Congress or the courts), its coverage is irrevocable. To hold that the political branches may switch the Constitution on or off at will would lead to a regime in which they, not this Court, say "what the law is".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Puerto Ricans "were collectively made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._citizenship#Birthright_citizenship" title="U.S. citizenship">U.S. citizens</a>" in 1917 as a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jones-Shafroth_Act" title="Jones-Shafroth Act">Jones-Shafroth Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[162]</a></sup> U.S. citizens residing in Puerto Rico cannot vote for the U.S. president, though both major parties, Republican and Democratic, run primary elections in Puerto Rico to send delegates to vote on a presidential candidate. Since Puerto Rico is an <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_territories_of_the_United_States" title="Unincorporated territories of the United States">unincorporated territory</a> (see above) and not a U.S. state, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> does not fully <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfranchise" title="Enfranchise">enfranchise</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_citizen" title="US citizen">US citizens</a> residing in Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Department_of_State_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Department_of_State-155">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitutional_Topic:_Citizenship_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Constitutional_Topic:_Citizenship-176">[163]</a></sup> (<i>See also:</i> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_rights_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Voting rights in Puerto Rico">Voting rights in Puerto Rico</a>").</p>
<p>Only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_right" title="Fundamental right">fundamental rights</a> under the American federal constitution and adjudications are applied to Puerto Ricans. Various other U.S Supreme Court decisions have held which rights apply in Puerto Rico and which ones do not. Puerto Ricans have a long history of service in the U.S. Armed Forces and, since 1917, they have been included in the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compulsory_draft" title="Compulsory draft">compulsory draft</a> whensoever it has been in effect.</p>
<p>Though the Commonwealth government has its own tax laws, Puerto Ricans are also required to pay many kinds of U.S. federal taxes, not including the federal personal income tax for Puerto Rico-sourced income, but only under certain circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters2009-09-24_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters2009-09-24-180">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jct_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-jct-183">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[171]</a></sup> In 2009, Puerto Rico paid <span class="nowrap">$3.742 billion</span> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Treasury" title="US Treasury">US Treasury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[172]</a></sup> Residents of Puerto Rico pay into Social Security, and are thus eligible for Social Security benefits upon retirement. They are excluded from the <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Security_Income" title="Supplemental Security Income">Supplemental Security Income</a> (SSI), and the island actually receives a smaller fraction of the <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a> funding it would receive if it were a U.S. state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[173]</a></sup> Also, Medicare providers receive less-than-full state-like reimbursements for services rendered to beneficiaries in Puerto Rico, even though the latter paid fully into the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>While a state may try an individual for the same crime he/she was tried in federal court, this is not the case in Puerto Rico. Being a US territory, Puerto Rico's authority to enact a criminal code derives from Congress and not from local sovereignty as with the states. Thus, such a parallel accusation would constitute double jeopardy and is constitutionally impermissible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mart.C3.ADnez_Torres_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mart.C3.ADnez_Torres-162">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992, President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> issued a memorandum to heads of executive departments and agencies establishing the current administrative relationship between the federal government and the Commonwealth of Puerto Rico. This memorandum directs all federal departments, agencies, and officials to treat Puerto Rico administratively as if it were a state, insofar as doing so would not disrupt federal programs or operations.</p>
<p>Many federal executive branch agencies have significant presence in Puerto Rico, just as in any state, including the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Management_Agency" title="Federal Emergency Management Agency">Federal Emergency Management Agency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transportation_Security_Administration" title="Transportation Security Administration">Transportation Security Administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_Administration" title="Social Security Administration">Social Security Administration</a>, and others. While Puerto Rico has its own <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Judiciary of Puerto Rico">Commonwealth judicial system</a> similar to that of a U.S. state, there is also a U.S federal district court in Puerto Rico, and Puerto Ricans have served as judges in that Court and in other federal courts on the U.S. mainland regardless of their residency status at the time of their appointment. <a href="/wiki/Sonia_Sotomayor" title="Sonia Sotomayor">Sonia Sotomayor</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nuyorican" title="Nuyorican">New Yorker of Puerto Rican descent</a>, serves as an <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice" title="Associate Justice">Associate Justice</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>. Puerto Ricans have also been frequently appointed to high-level federal positions, including serving as <a href="/wiki/Ambassadors_of_the_United_States" title="Ambassadors of the United States">United States Ambassadors</a> to other nations.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_status">International status</span></h4>
<p>On November 27, 1953, shortly after the establishment of the Commonwealth, the General Assembly of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> approved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly_Resolution" title="UN General Assembly Resolution">Resolution 748</a>, removing Puerto Rico's classification as a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_list_of_Non-Self-Governing_Territories" title="United Nations list of Non-Self-Governing Territories">non-self-governing territory</a>. The General Assembly did not apply the full list of criteria which was enunciated in 1960 when it took favorable note of the cessation of transmission of information regarding the non-self-governing status of Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the White House Task Force on Puerto Rico's Political Status in its December 21, 2007 report, the U.S., in its written submission to the UN in 1953, never represented that Congress could not change its relationship with Puerto Rico without the territory's consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-status2007_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-status2007-190">[177]</a></sup> It stated that the U.S. Justice Department in 1959 reiterated that Congress held power over Puerto Rico pursuant to the Territorial Clause<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artice4_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Artice4-191">[178]</a></sup> of the U.S. Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-status2007_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-status2007-190">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Eleventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit</a> stated that Congress may unilaterally repeal the Puerto Rican Constitution or the Puerto Rican Federal Relations Act and replace them with any rules or regulations of its choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ftp.resources.com_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-ftp.resources.com-192">[179]</a></sup> In a 1996 report on a Puerto Rico status political bill, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Natural_Resources" title="United States House Committee on Natural Resources">U.S. House Committee on Resources</a> stated, "Puerto Rico's current status does not meet the criteria for any of the options for full self-government under Resolution 1541" (the three established forms of full self-government being stated in the report as (1) national independence, (2) free association based on separate sovereignty, or (3) full integration with another nation on the basis of equality). The report concluded that Puerto Rico "... remains an unincorporated colony and does not have the status of 'free association' with the United States as that status is defined under United States law or international practice", that the establishment of local self-government with the consent of the people can be unilaterally revoked by the U.S. Congress, and that U.S. Congress can also withdraw the U.S. citizenship of Puerto Rican residents of Puerto Rico at any time, for a legitimate Federal purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[181]</a></sup> The application of the U.S. Constitution to Puerto Rico is limited by the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Cases" title="Insular Cases">Insular Cases</a>.</p>
<p>In 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[182]</a></sup> 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[183]</a></sup> 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[184]</a></sup> 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[185]</a></sup> and 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[186]</a></sup> the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Special_Committee_on_Decolonization" title="Special Committee on Decolonization">Special Committee on Decolonization</a> passed resolutions calling on the United States to expedite a process "that would allow Puerto Ricans to fully exercise their inalienable right to self-determination and independence",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_decolonization_committee_eyes_PR_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_decolonization_committee_eyes_PR-200">[187]</a></sup> and to release all Puerto Rican political prisoners in U.S. prisons, to clean up, decontaminate and return the lands in the islands of Vieques and Culebra to the people of Puerto Rico, to perform a probe into U.S. human rights violations on the island and a probe into the killing by the FBI of pro-independence leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filiberto_Ojeda_Rios" title="Filiberto Ojeda Rios">Filiberto Ojeda Rios</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_developments">Recent developments</span></h4>
