<td colspan="5" style="padding:5px">There were 515,348 blank and invalidated ballots counted alongside the 1,363,854 ballots which indicated a choice for one of the non-territorial alternatives. Under Puerto Rico Law, these ballots are not considered cast votes and are therefore not reflected in the final tally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oslpr.org_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-oslpr.org-201">[188]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On July 15, 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Special_Committee_on_Decolonization" title="Special Committee on Decolonization">United Nations Special Committee on Decolonization</a> approved a draft resolution calling on the Government of the United States to expedite a process that would allow the Puerto Rican people to exercise fully their inalienable right to self-determination and independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 29, 2010, the U.S. House voted 223–169 to approve a measure for a federally sanctioned process for Puerto Rico's self-determination, allowing Puerto Rico to set a new referendum on whether to continue its present form of commonwealth, or to have a different political status. If Puerto Ricans voted to continue as a commonwealth, the Government of Puerto Rico was authorized to conduct additional plebiscites at intervals of every eight years from the date on which the results of the prior plebiscite were certified; if Puerto Ricans voted to have a different political status, a second referendum would determine whether Puerto Rico would become a U.S. state, an independent country, or a sovereign nation associated with the U.S. that would not be subject to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_Clause" title="Territorial Clause">Territorial Clause</a> of the United States Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afp-referendum_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-afp-referendum-203">[190]</a></sup> During the House debate, a fourth option, to retain its present form of commonwealth (sometimes referred to as "the <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">status quo</a>") political status, was added as an option in the second plebiscite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afp-referendum_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-afp-referendum-203">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Immediately following <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House</a> passage, H.R. 2499 was sent to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a>, where it was given two formal readings and referred to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Energy_and_Natural_Resources" title="United States Senate Committee on Energy and Natural Resources">Senate Committee on Energy and Natural Resources</a>. On December 22, 2010, the 111th United States Congress adjourned without any Senate vote on H.R.2499, killing the bill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>The latest Task Force report was released on March 11, 2011. The report suggested a two-plebiscite process, including a "first plebiscite that requires the people of Puerto Rico to choose whether they wish to be part of the United States (either via Statehood or Commonwealth) or wish to be independent (via Independence or Free Association). If continuing to be part of the United States were chosen in the first plebiscite, a second vote would be taken between Statehood and Commonwealth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-President_Task_Force_Status_Report_2011_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-President_Task_Force_Status_Report_2011-206">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 14, 2011, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> "promised to support 'a clear decision' by the people of Puerto Rico on statehood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[194]</a></sup> That same month, the United Nations Special Committee on Decolonization passed a resolution and adopted a consensus text introduced by Cuba's delegate on June 20, 2011, calling on the United States to expedite a process "that would allow Puerto Ricans to fully exercise their inalienable right to self-determination and independence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_decolonization_committee_eyes_PR_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN_decolonization_committee_eyes_PR-200">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 6, 2012, a two-question referendum took place, simultaneous with the general elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oslpr1_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-oslpr1-208">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oslpr1_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-oslpr1-208">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[196]</a></sup> The first question asked voters whether they wanted to maintain the current status under the territorial clause of the U.S. Constitution. The second question posed three alternate status options if the first question was approved: statehood, independence or <a href="/wiki/Compact_of_Free_Association" title="Compact of Free Association">free association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[197]</a></sup> For the first question, 54 percent voted against the current Commonwealth status. For the second question, 61.16% voted for statehood, 33.34% for a sovereign free associated state, and 5.49% for independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were also 515,348 blank and invalidated ballots, which are not reflected in the final tally, as they are not considered cast votes under Puerto Rico law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oslpr.org_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-oslpr.org-201">[188]</a></sup> On December 11, 2012, Puerto Rico's Legislature passed a concurrent resolution to request to the President and the U.S. Congress action on November 6, 2012 plebiscite results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[199]</a></sup> But on April 10, 2013, with the issue still being widely debated, the White House announced that it will seek $2.5 million to hold another referendum, this next one being the first Puerto Rican status referendum to be financed by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. Federal government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap_referendum_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap_referendum-213">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015, the U.S. Government submitted a brief as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amicus_Curiae" title="Amicus Curiae">Amicus Curiae</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> related to the case Commonwealth of Puerto Rico v. Sanchez Valle. The U.S. Government official position is that the U.S. Constitution does not contemplate "sovereign territories". That the Court has consistently recognized that "there is no sovereignty in a Territory of the United States but that of the United States itself". and a U.S. territory has "no independent sovereignty comparable to that of a state. That is because "the Government of a territory owes its existence wholly to the United States". Congress's plenary authority over federal territories includes the authority to permit self-government, whereby local officials administer a territory's internal affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 9, 2016, in Commonwealth of Puerto Rico vs Sanchez Valle, an 6–2 majority of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> determined that Puerto Rico is a territory and lacks Sovereignty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Commonwealth_of_Puerto_Rico_v._Sanchez_Valle_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Commonwealth_of_Puerto_Rico_v._Sanchez_Valle-215">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 30, 2016, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="President of the United States of America">President of the United States of America</a> signed a new law approved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a>, H.R. 5278: PROMESA, establishing a Control Board over the Puerto Rico Government. This board will have a significant degree of federal control involved in its establishment and operations. In particular, the authority to establish the control board derives from the federal government's constitutional power to "make all needful rules and regulations" regarding U.S. territories; The President would appoint all seven voting members of the board; and the board would have broad sovereign powers to effectively overrule decisions by Puerto Rico's legislature, governor, and other public authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_and_intergovernmental_relations">Foreign and intergovernmental relations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_intergovernmental_relations_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Foreign and intergovernmental relations of Puerto Rico">Foreign and intergovernmental relations of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>Puerto Rico is subject to the <a href="/wiki/Commerce_Clause" title="Commerce Clause">Commerce</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_Clause" title="Territorial Clause">Territorial Clause</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a> and, therefore, is restricted on how it can engage with other nations, sharing the opportunities and limitations that state governments have albeit not being one. As is the case with state governments, regardless, it has established several <a href="/wiki/Trade_agreement" title="Trade agreement">trade agreements</a> with other nations, particularly with <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">Hispanic American</a> countries such as <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panam%C3%A1" title="Panamá">Panamá</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has also established trade promotion offices in many foreign countries, all Spanish-speaking, and within the United States itself, which now include <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, and has included in the past offices in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Such agreements require permission from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" title="U.S. Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a>; most, are simply allowed by existing laws or trade treaties between the United States and other nations which supersede trade agreements pursued by Puerto Rico and different U.S. states.</p>
<p>At the local level, Puerto Rico established by law that the <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> which states and territories are allowed to engage must be handled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_State_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Department of State of Puerto Rico">Department of State of Puerto Rico</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Executive_departments_of_the_government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Executive departments of the government of Puerto Rico">executive department</a>, headed by the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Secretary of State of Puerto Rico">Secretary of State of Puerto Rico</a>, who also serves as the territory's lieutenant governor. It is also charged to liaise with general <a href="/wiki/Consul" title="Consul">consuls</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorary_consul" title="Honorary consul">honorary consuls</a> based in Puerto Rico. The <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Federal_Affairs_Administration" title="Puerto Rico Federal Affairs Administration">Puerto Rico Federal Affairs Administration</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Resident Commissioner of Puerto Rico">Office of the Resident Commissioner</a>, manage all its intergovernmental affairs before entities of or in the United States (including the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_the_United_States" title="Local government in the United States">local</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_governments_of_the_United_States" title="State governments of the United States">state governments of the United States</a>, and public or private entities in the United States).</p>
<p>Both entities frequently assist the Department of State of Puerto Rico in engaging with Washington, D.C.-based ambassadors and federal agencies that handle Puerto Rico's foreign affairs, such as the U.S. Department of State, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agency_for_International_Development" title="Agency for International Development">Agency for International Development</a>, and others. The current <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Secretary of State of Puerto Rico">Secretary of State</a> is <a href="/wiki/V%C3%ADctor_Su%C3%A1rez_Mel%C3%A9ndez" title="Víctor Suárez Meléndez">Víctor Suárez Meléndez</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Popular_Democratic_Party_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Popular Democratic Party (Puerto Rico)">Popular Democratic Party</a> and member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party of the United States</a>, while the current <a href="/wiki/List_of_Directors_of_the_Puerto_Rico_Federal_Affairs_Administration" title="List of Directors of the Puerto Rico Federal Affairs Administration">Director of the Puerto Rico Federal Affairs Administration</a> is <a href="/wiki/Juan_Eugenio_Hern%C3%A1ndez_Mayoral" title="Juan Eugenio Hernández Mayoral">Juan Eugenio Hernández Mayoral</a> also from the Popular Democratic and member of the Democratic Party.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Resident_Commissioner_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Resident Commissioner of Puerto Rico">Resident Commissioner of Puerto Rico</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delegate_(United_States_Congress)" title="Delegate (United States Congress)">delegate</a> elected by Puerto Ricans to represent them before the federal government, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a>, sits in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>, serves and votes on congressional committees, and functions in every respect as a legislator except being denied a vote on the final disposition of legislation on the House floor, also engages in foreign affairs to the same extent as other members of Congress. The current Resident Commissioner is <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Pierluisi" title="Pedro Pierluisi">Pedro Pierluisi</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Progressive_Party_of_Puerto_Rico" title="New Progressive Party of Puerto Rico">New Progressive Party</a> and member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party of the United States</a>.</p>
<p>Many Puerto Ricans have served as United States ambassadors to different nations and international organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a>, mostly but not exclusively in Latin America. For example, Maricarmen Aponte, a Puerto Rican and now an Acting Assistant Secretary of State, previously served as U.S. ambassador to <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Military of Puerto Rico">Military of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>As it is a territory of the United States of America, the defense of Puerto Rico is provided by the United States as part of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">Treaty of Paris</a> with the President of the United States as its <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a>. Puerto Rico has its own <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_National_Guard" title="Puerto Rico National Guard">Puerto Rico National Guard</a>, and its own <a href="/wiki/State_defense_force" title="State defense force">state defense force</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_State_Guard" title="Puerto Rico State Guard">Puerto Rico State Guard</a>, which by local law is under the authority of the Puerto Rico National Guard.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of both local forces is the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Governor of Puerto Rico">governor of Puerto Rico</a> who delegates his authority to the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Adjutant_General" title="Puerto Rico Adjutant General">Puerto Rico Adjutant General</a>, currently <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">Colonel</a> <a href="/wiki/Marta_Carcana" title="Marta Carcana">Marta Carcana</a>. The Adjutant General, in turn, delegates the authority over the State Guard to another officer but retains the authority over the Puerto Rico National Guard as a whole.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="1312" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG/800px-Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG/1200px-Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG 2x" width="800"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Federal_lands_in_Puerto_Rico_and_VI.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. military installations in Puerto Rico (including the United States Virgin Islands) throughout the 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>U.S. military installations in Puerto Rico were part of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atlantic_Command" title="United States Atlantic Command">U.S. Atlantic Command</a> (LANTCOM after 1993 USACOM), which had authority over all US military operations that took place throughout the Atlantic. Puerto Rico had been seen as crucial in supporting LANTCOM's mission until 1999, when U.S. Atlantic Command was renamed and given a new mission as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Joint_Forces_Command" title="United States Joint Forces Command">United States Joint Forces Command</a>. Puerto Rico is currently under the responsibility of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Northern_Command" title="United States Northern Command">United States Northern Command</a>.</p>
<p>Both the Naval Forces Caribbean (NFC) and the Fleet Air Caribbean (FAIR) were formerly based at the Roosevelt Roads Naval Station. The NFC had authority over all US Naval activity in the waters of the Caribbean while FAIR had authority over all US military flights and air operations over the Caribbean. With the closing of the Roosevelt Roads and Vieques Island training facilities, the US Navy has basically exited from Puerto Rico, except for the ships that steam by, and the only significant military presence in the island is the U.S. Army at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Buchanan,_Puerto_Rico" title="Fort Buchanan, Puerto Rico">Ft Buchanan</a>, the Puerto Rican Army and Air National Guards, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Coast_Guard" title="U.S. Coast Guard">U.S. Coast Guard</a>.</p>
<p>A branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Army_National_Guard" title="United States Army National Guard">U.S. Army National Guard</a> is stationed in Puerto Rico — known as the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Army_National_Guard" title="Puerto Rico Army National Guard">Puerto Rico Army National Guard</a> — which performs missions equivalent to those of the Army National Guards of the different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_the_United_States" title="States of the United States">states of the United States</a>, including ground defense, disaster relief, and control of civil unrest. The local National Guard also incorporates a branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Air_National_Guard" title="United States Air National Guard">U.S. Air National Guard</a> — known as the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Air_National_Guard" title="Puerto Rico Air National Guard">Puerto Rico Air National Guard</a> — which performs missions equivalent to those of the Air National Guards of each one of the U.S. states.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Maryland_(SSBN-738)_1997.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1400" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg/300px-USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg/450px-USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg/600px-USS_Maryland_%28SSBN-738%29_1997.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Maryland_(SSBN-738)_1997.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ohio-class_submarine" title="Ohio-class submarine"><i>Ohio</i>-class</a> <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarine</a> USS <i>Maryland</i>, <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Roads_Naval_Station" title="Roosevelt Roads Naval Station">Roosevelt Roads Naval Station</a>, 1997</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At different times in the 20th century, the U.S. had about 25 military or naval installations in Puerto Rico, some very small ones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WQS_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-WQS-220">[207]</a></sup> as well as large installations. The largest of these installations were the former <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Roads_Naval_Station" title="Roosevelt Roads Naval Station">Roosevelt Roads Naval Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ceiba,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ceiba, Puerto Rico">Ceiba</a>, the Atlantic Fleet Weapons Training Facility (AFWTF) on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vieques" title="Vieques">Vieques</a>, the National Guard training facility at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camp_Santiago" title="Camp Santiago">Camp Santiago</a> in <a href="/wiki/Salinas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Salinas, Puerto Rico">Salinas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Allen,_Puerto_Rico" title="Fort Allen, Puerto Rico">Fort Allen</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juana_Diaz" title="Juana Diaz">Juana Diaz</a>, the Army's <a href="/wiki/Fort_Buchanan,_Puerto_Rico" title="Fort Buchanan, Puerto Rico">Fort Buchanan</a> in San Juan, the former U.S. Air Force Ramey Air Force Base in Aguadilla, and the Puerto Rico Air National Guard at Muñiz Air Force base in <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD-221">[208]</a></sup></p>
<p>The former U.S. Navy facilities at Roosevelt Roads, Vieques, and Sabana Seca have been deactivated and partially turned over to the local government. Other than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Coast_Guard" title="U.S. Coast Guard">U.S. Coast Guard</a> and Puerto Rico National Guard facilities, there are only two remaining military installations in Puerto Rico: the U.S. Army's small Ft. Buchanan (supporting local veterans and reserve units) and the PRANG (Puerto Rico Air National Guard) Muñiz Air Base (the C-130 Fleet). In recent years, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a> has considered their deactivations, but these have been opposed by diverse public and private entities in Puerto Rico – such as retired military who rely on Ft. Buchanan for the services available there.</p>
<p>Puerto Ricans have participated in many of the military conflicts in which the United States has been involved. For example, they participated in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, when volunteers from Puerto Rico, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> fought the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">British</a> in 1779 under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_de_G%C3%A1lvez" title="Bernardo de Gálvez">Bernardo de Gálvez</a> (1746–1786),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[209]</a></sup> and have continued to participate up to the present-day conflicts in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>A significant number of Puerto Ricans participate as members and work for the U.S. Armed Services, largely as <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_the_United_States" title="National Guard of the United States">National Guard</a> members and civilian employees. The size of the overall military-related community in Puerto Rico is estimated to be 100,000 individuals. This includes retired personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-CD-221">[208]</a></sup> Fort Buchanan has about 4,000 military and civilian personnel. In addition, approximately 17,000 people are members of the Puerto Rico Army and Air National Guards, or the U.S. Reserve forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSD_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSD-224">[211]</a></sup> Puerto Rican soldiers have served in every US military conflict from <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> to the current military engagement known by the United States and its allies as the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War against Terrorism</a>.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/65th_Infantry_Regiment" title="65th Infantry Regiment">65th Infantry Regiment</a>, nicknamed "<i>The Borinqueneers</i>" from the original Taíno name of the island (Borinquen), is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_people" title="Puerto Rican people">Puerto Rican</a> regiment of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>. The regiment's motto is <i>Honor et Fidelitas</i>, Latin for <i>Honor and Fidelity</i>. The 65th Infantry Regiment participated in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a> and in 2014 was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>, presented by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, for its heroism during the Korean War.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Municipalities of Puerto Rico">Municipalities of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>As an unincorporated territory of the United States, Puerto Rico does not have any first order administrative divisions as defined by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Government" title="U.S. Government">U.S. Government</a>, but there are 78 <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Municipalities of Puerto Rico">municipalities</a> at the secondary level which function as counties. Municipalities are further subdivided into <i><a href="/wiki/Barrio" title="Barrio">barrios</a></i>, and those into sectors. Each municipality has a <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">mayor</a> and a municipal legislature elected to four-year terms.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="1058" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg/800px-USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg/1200px-USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg/1600px-USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg.png 2x" width="800"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA_Puerto_Rico_labeled.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of Puerto Rico showing its municipalities</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Economy of Puerto Rico">Economy of Puerto Rico</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_government-debt_crisis" title="Puerto Rico government-debt crisis">Puerto Rico government-debt crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1560" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png/350px-Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png/525px-Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png/700px-Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puerto-rico-gdp-by-sector.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Puerto Rico's <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) by <a href="/wiki/Economic_sector" title="Economic sector">economic sector</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The economy of Puerto Rico is classified as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_income_economy" title="High income economy">high income economy</a> by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and as the most competitive economy in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> by the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> but Puerto Rico currently has a public debt of $72.204 billion (equivalent to 103% of GNP), and a government deficit of $2.5 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wef-gcr-2013_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-wef-gcr-2013-226">[213]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_income" title="Gross national income">gross national income</a> per capita of Puerto Rico in 2013 is $23,830 (PPP, International Dollars), ranked as 63rd among all sovereign and dependent territories entities in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[214]</a></sup> Its economy is mainly driven by <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Manufacturing in Puerto Rico">manufacturing</a> (primarily pharmaceuticals, textiles, petrochemicals and electronics) followed by the service industry (primarily finance, insurance, <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Real estate in Puerto Rico">real estate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Tourism in Puerto Rico">tourism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[n]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[o]</a></sup> In recent years, the territory has also become a popular destination for MICE (<a href="/wiki/Meetings,_incentives,_conferencing,_exhibitions" title="Meetings, incentives, conferencing, exhibitions">meetings, incentives, conferencing, exhibitions</a>), with a modern convention centre district overlooking the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_San_Juan" title="Port of San Juan">Port of San Juan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Geography of Puerto Rico">geography of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_status_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Political status of Puerto Rico">its political status</a> are both determining factors on its economic prosperity, primarily due to its relatively small size as an island; <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource_economics" title="Natural resource economics">its lack of natural resources</a> used to produce <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">raw materials</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, and, consequently, its dependence on <a href="/wiki/Import" title="Import">imports</a>; as well as its territorial status with the United States, which controls its <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy" title="Foreign policy">foreign policy</a> while exerting trading restrictions, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Transportation in Puerto Rico">its shipping industry</a>.</p>
<p>Puerto Rico experienced a recession from 2006 to 2011, interrupted by 4 quarters of economic growth, and entered into recession again in 2013, following growing fiscal imbalance and the expiration of the IRS Section 936 corporate incentives that the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Code" title="Internal Revenue Code">U.S. Internal Revenue Code</a> had applied to Puerto Rico. This IRS section was critical to the economy, as it established <a href="/wiki/Tax_exemption" title="Tax exemption">tax exemptions</a> for U.S. corporations that settled in Puerto Rico, and allowed their insular subsidiaries to send their earnings to the parent corporation at any time, without paying federal tax on corporate income. Puerto Rico has surprisingly been able to maintain a relatively low inflation in the past decade while maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> per capita higher than 80% of the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>Academically, most of Puerto Rico's economic woes stem from federal regulations that expired, have been repealed, or no longer apply to Puerto Rico; its inability to become self-sufficient and self-sustainable throughout history;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[p]</a></sup> its highly politicized public policy which tends to change <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Political party strength in Puerto Rico">whenever a political party gains power</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[q]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Government of Puerto Rico">its highly inefficient local government</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[r]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[s]</a></sup> which has accrued a <a href="/wiki/Public_debt_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Public debt of Puerto Rico">public debt</a> equal to 68% of its <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> throughout time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[t]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[u]</a></sup></p>
<p>In comparison to <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">the different states of the United States</a>, Puerto Rico is poorer than Mississippi (the poorest state of the U.S.) with 41% of its population below the <a href="/wiki/Poverty_threshold" title="Poverty threshold">poverty line</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[v]</a></sup> When compared to Latin America, Puerto Rico has the highest GDP per capita in the region. Its main trading partners are the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> itself, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, with most products coming from <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, mainly from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. At a global scale, Puerto Rico's dependency on oil for <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Transportation in Puerto Rico">transportation</a> and electricity generation, as well as its dependency on food imports and raw materials, makes Puerto Rico volatile and highly reactive to changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_economy" title="Global economy">the world economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>. Puerto Rico's agricultural sector represents less than 1% of GNP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[224]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heavy_debt_load">Heavy debt load</span></h3>
<p>In early 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_government-debt_crisis" title="Puerto Rican government-debt crisis">Puerto Rican government-debt crisis</a> posed serious problems for the government which was saddled with outstanding bond debt that had climbed to $70 billion at a time with a 45 percent poverty rate and 12.4% unemployment that is more than twice the mainland U.S. average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[225]</a></sup> The debt had been increasing during a decade long recession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-30">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Commonwealth had been defaulting on many debts, including bonds, since 2015. With debt payments due, the Governor was facing the risk of a government shutdown and failure to fund the managed health care system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-249">[227]</a></sup> "Without action before April, Puerto Rico’s ability to execute contracts for Fiscal Year 2018 with its managed care organizations will be threatened, thereby putting at risk beginning July 1, 2017 the health care of up to 900,000 poor U.S. citizens living in Puerto Rico", according to a letter sent to Congress by the Secretary of the Treasury and the Secretary of Health and Human Services. They also said that "Congress must enact measures recommended by both Republicans and Democrats that fix Puerto Rico’s inequitable health care financing structure and promote sustained economic growth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-249">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, the oversight board created under <a href="/wiki/PROMESA" title="PROMESA">PROMESA</a> called for Puerto Rico's governor <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Rossell%C3%B3" title="Ricardo Rosselló">Ricardo Rosselló</a> to deliver a fiscal turnaround plan by January 28. Just before that deadline, the control board gave the Commonwealth government until February 28 to present a fiscal plan (including negotiations with creditors for restructuring debt) to solve the problems. A moratorium on lawsuits by debtors was extended to May 31.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-30">[27]</a></sup> It is essential for Puerto Rico to reach restructuring deals to avoid a bankruptcy-like process under <a href="/wiki/PROMESA" title="PROMESA">PROMESA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[228]</a></sup> An internal survey conducted by the Puerto Rican Economists Association revealed that the majority of Puerto Rican economists reject the policy recommendations of the Board and the Rosselló government, with more than 80% of economists arguing in favor of auditing the debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early August 2017, the island's financial oversight board (created by PROMESA) planned to institute two days off without pay per month for government employees, down from the original plan of four days per month; the latter had been expected to achieve $218 million in savings. Governor Rossello rejected this plan as unjustified and unnecessary. Pension reforms were also discussed including a proposal for a 10% reduction in benefits to begin addressing the $50 billion in unfunded pension liabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters.com_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Transportation in Puerto Rico">Transportation in Puerto Rico</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communications_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Communications in Puerto Rico">Communications in Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Electric_Power_Authority" title="Puerto Rico Electric Power Authority">Puerto Rico Electric Power Authority</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Aqueducts_and_Sewers_Authority" title="Puerto Rico Aqueducts and Sewers Authority">Puerto Rico Aqueducts and Sewers Authority</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="283" data-file-width="675" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg/350px-Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg/525px-Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg/700px-Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puerto_Rico_Interstates.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/List_of_highways_in_Puerto_Rico" title="List of highways in Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico interstate highways</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cities and towns in Puerto Rico are interconnected by a system of roads, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">freeways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limited-access_road" title="Limited-access road">expressways</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Highway" title="Highway">highways</a> maintained by the Highways and Transportation Authority under the jurisdiction of the U.S. Department of Transportation, and patrolled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Police_Department" title="Puerto Rico Police Department">Puerto Rico Police Department</a>. The island's <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan area</a> is served by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoridad_Metropolitana_de_Autobuses" title="Autoridad Metropolitana de Autobuses">public bus transit system</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">metro system</a> called <i><a href="/wiki/Tren_Urbano" title="Tren Urbano">Tren Urbano</a></i> (in English: Urban Train). Other forms of public transportation include seaborne ferries (that serve Puerto Rico's archipelago) as well as <i>Carros Públicos</i> (<a href="/wiki/Share_taxi" title="Share taxi">private mini buses</a>).</p>
<p>Puerto Rico has three <a href="/wiki/International_airport" title="International airport">international airports</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Luis_Mu%C3%B1oz_Mar%C3%ADn_International_Airport" title="Luis Muñoz Marín International Airport">Luis Muñoz Marín International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carolina,_Puerto_Rico" title="Carolina, Puerto Rico">Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercedita_Airport" title="Mercedita Airport">Mercedita Airport</a> in Ponce, and the <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Hern%C3%A1ndez_Airport" title="Rafael Hernández Airport">Rafael Hernández Airport</a> in Aguadilla, and 27 local airports. The Luis Muñoz Marín International Airport is the largest aerial transportation hub in the Caribbean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRPA2008-07-28_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-PRPA2008-07-28-253">[231]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_(Puerto_Rico).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg/300px-Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg/450px-Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg/600px-Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_%28Puerto_Rico%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tren_Urbano_in_Bayam%C3%B3n_(Puerto_Rico).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tren_Urbano" title="Tren Urbano">Tren Urbano</a> system at Bayamón Station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Puerto Rico has nine <a href="/wiki/Port" title="Port">ports</a> in different cities across the main island. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Juan_Port" title="San Juan Port">San Juan Port</a> is the largest in Puerto Rico, and the busiest port in the Caribbean and the 10th busiest in the United States in terms of commercial activity and cargo movement, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRPA2008-07-28_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-PRPA2008-07-28-253">[231]</a></sup> The second largest port is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_the_Americas" title="Port of the Americas">Port of the Americas</a> in Ponce, currently under expansion to increase cargo capacity to <span class="nowrap">1.5 million</span> twenty-foot containers (<a href="/wiki/Twenty-foot_equivalent_unit" title="Twenty-foot equivalent unit">TEUs</a>) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <b>Puerto Rico Electric Power Authority</b> (<b>PREPA</b>)—Spanish: <i>Autoridad de Energía Eléctrica</i> (<b>AEE</b>)—is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_power_company" title="Electric power company">electric power company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_government-owned_corporations_of_Puerto_Rico" title="List of government-owned corporations of Puerto Rico">government-owned corporation of Puerto Rico</a> responsible for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">power transmission</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_distribution" title="Electric power distribution">power distribution</a> in Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[233]</a></sup> PREPA is the only entity authorized to conduct such business in Puerto Rico, effectively making it a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_monopoly" title="Government monopoly">government monopoly</a>. The Authority is ruled by a Governing Board appointed by the Governor with the <a href="/wiki/Advice_and_consent" title="Advice and consent">advice and consent</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Senate of Puerto Rico">Senate of Puerto Rico</a>, and is run by an Executive Director.</p>
<p>Telecommunications in Puerto Rico includes radio, television, fixed and mobile telephones, and the Internet. Broadcasting in Puerto Rico is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">US Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[234]</a></sup> As of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 30 TV stations, 125 radio stations and roughly 1 million TV sets on the island. Cable TV subscription services are available and the <a href="/wiki/American_Forces_Network" title="American Forces Network">US Armed Forces Radio and Television Service</a> also broadcast on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-PuertoRico-2013_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-PuertoRico-2013-257">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_finances">Public finances</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_government-debt_crisis" title="Puerto Rico government-debt crisis">Puerto Rico government-debt crisis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budget_of_the_Government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Budget of the Government of Puerto Rico">Budget of the Government of Puerto Rico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Public_debt_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Public debt of Puerto Rico">public debt of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>Puerto Rico has an <a href="/wiki/Operating_budget" title="Operating budget">operating budget</a> of about US$9.8 billion with expenses at about $10.4 billion; creating a structural deficit of $775 million (about 7.9% of the budget).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-el-vocero-la-estadidad-es-uniforme_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-el-vocero-la-estadidad-es-uniforme-258">[236]</a></sup> The practice of approving budgets with a structural deficit has been done for 17 consecutive years starting in 2000. Throughout those years, including present time, all budgets contemplated issuing bonds to cover said projected deficits rather than make proper adjustments. This practice eroded Puerto Rico's treasury as the government had already been issuing bonds <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_government_budget_balance" title="Puerto Rico government budget balance">to balance its actual budget</a> for four decades since 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[w]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[238]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1185" data-file-width="1469" height="323" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png/400px-Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png/600px-Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png/800px-Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Budget-of-the-government-of-puerto-rico-2012-percentage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2012 Budget of the Government of Puerto Rico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Projected deficits added substantial burdens to an already indebted nation which accrued <a href="/wiki/Public_debt_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Public debt of Puerto Rico">a public debt</a> of $71B or about 70% of Puerto Rico's gross domestic product. This sparked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_government-debt_crisis" title="Puerto Rico government-debt crisis">an ongoing government-debt crisis</a> after Puerto Rico's general obligation bonds were downgraded to speculative non-investment grade ("junk status") by three credit rating agencies. In terms of financial control, almost 9.6%—or about $1.5 billion—of Puerto Rico's central government budget expenses for FY2014 is expected to be spent on debt service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[x]</a></sup> Harsher budget cuts are expected as Puerto Rico must now repay larger chunks of debts in the following years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (July 2016)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>For practical reasons the budget is divided into two aspects: a "general budget" which comprises the assignments funded exclusively by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Treasury_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Department of Treasury of Puerto Rico">Department of Treasury of Puerto Rico</a>, and the "consolidated budget" which comprises the assignments funded by the general budget, by <a href="/wiki/List_of_government-owned_corporations_of_Puerto_Rico" title="List of government-owned corporations of Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico's government-owned corporations</a>, by revenue expected from loans, by the sale of government bonds, by subsidies extended by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government of the United States</a>, and by other funds.</p>
<p>Both budgets contrast each other drastically, with the consolidated budget being usually thrice the size of the general budget; currently $29B and $9.0B respectively. Almost one out of every four dollars in the consolidated budget comes from U.S. federal subsidies while government-owned corporations compose more than 31% of the consolidated budget.</p>
<p>The critical aspects come from the sale of bonds, which comprise 7% of the consolidated budget; a ratio that increased annually due to the government's inability to prepare a balanced budget in addition to being incapable of generating enough income to cover all its expenses. In particular, the government-owned corporations add a heavy burden to the overall budget and public debt as not a single one is self-sufficient, all of them carrying extremely inefficient operations. For example, in FY2011 the government-owned corporations reported aggregated losses of more than $1.3B with the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Highways_and_Transportation_Authority" title="Puerto Rico Highways and Transportation Authority">Puerto Rico Highways and Transportation Authority</a> (PRHTA) reporting losses of $409M, the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Electric_Power_Authority" title="Puerto Rico Electric Power Authority">Puerto Rico Electric Power Authority</a> (PREPA; the government monopoly that controls all electricity on the island) reporting losses of $272M, while the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Aqueducts_and_Sewers_Authority" title="Puerto Rico Aqueducts and Sewers Authority">Puerto Rico Aqueducts and Sewers Authority</a> (PRASA; the government monopoly that controls all water utilities on the island) reported losses of $112M.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>Losses by government-owned corporations have been defrayed through the issuance of bonds compounding more than 40% of Puerto Rico's entire public debt today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[241]</a></sup> Holistically, from FY2000–FY2010 Puerto Rico's debt grew at a <a href="/wiki/Compound_annual_growth_rate" title="Compound annual growth rate">compound annual growth rate</a> (CAGR) of 9% while GDP remained stagnant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[242]</a></sup> This has not always provided a long-term solution. In early July 2017 for example, the Prepa power authority was effectively bankrupt after defaulting in a plan to restructure $9 billion in bond debt; the agency planned to seek Court protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of protocol, the governor, together with the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="Puerto Rico Office of Management and Budget">Puerto Rico Office of Management and Budget</a> (OGP in Spanish), formulates the budget he believes is required to operate all government branches for the ensuing fiscal year. He then submits this formulation as a budget request to the Puerto Rican legislature before July 1, the date established by law as the beginning of Puerto Rico's fiscal year. While the constitution establishes that the request must be submitted "at the beginning of each regular session", the request is typically submitted during the first week of May as the regular sessions of the legislature begin in January and it would be unpractical to submit a request so far ahead. Once submitted the budget is then approved by the legislature, typically with amendments, through a <a href="/wiki/Joint_resolution" title="Joint resolution">joint resolution</a> and referred back to the governor for his approval. The governor then either approves it or vetoes it. If vetoed the legislature can then either refer it back with amendments for the governor's approval, or approve it without the governor's consent by two-thirds of the bodies of each chamber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once approved the Department of Treasury disburses funds to the Office of Management and Budget which in turn disburses the funds to the respective agencies, all while the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Government_Development_Bank" title="Puerto Rico Government Development Bank">Puerto Rico Government Development Bank</a> (the government's intergovernmental bank) manages all related banking affairs including those related to the government-owned corporations.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_living">Cost of living</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="837" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png/350px-Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png/525px-Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png/700px-Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-jones-act-carrier-routes-for-puerto-rico.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Marine_Act_of_1920" title="Merchant Marine Act of 1920">Jones Act</a> carrier routes for Puerto Rico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cost of living in Puerto Rico is high and has increased over the past decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[y]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[252]</a></sup> San Juan's in particular is higher than <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> but lower than <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[253]</a></sup> One factor is housing prices which are comparable to <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, although property taxes are considerably lower than most places in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[z]</a></sup></p>
<p>Statistics used for cost of living sometimes do not take into account certain costs, such as the high cost of electricity, which has hovered in the 24¢ to 30¢ range per kilowatt/hour, two to three times the national average, increased travel costs for longer flights, additional shipping fees, and the loss of promotional participation opportunities for customers "outside the continental United States". While some online stores do offer free shipping on orders to Puerto Rico, many merchants exclude Hawaii, Alaska, Puerto Rico and other United States territories.</p>
<p>The household median income is stated as $19,350 and the mean income as $30,463 in the US Census Bureau's 2015 update. The report also indicates that 45.5% of individuals are below the poverty level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[255]</a></sup> The median home value in Puerto Rico ranges from US$100,000 to US$214,000, while the national median home value sits at $119,600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[aa]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most cited contributors to the high cost of living in Puerto Rico is the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Marine_Act_of_1920" title="Merchant Marine Act of 1920">Merchant Marine Act of 1920</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Marine_Act_of_1920" title="Merchant Marine Act of 1920">Jones Act</a>, which prevents foreign-flagged ships from carrying cargo between two American ports, a practice known as <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">cabotage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erg-trade_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-erg-trade-284">[257]</a></sup> Because of the Jones Act, foreign ships inbound with goods from <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> cannot stop in Puerto Rico, offload Puerto Rico-bound goods, load mainland-bound Puerto Rico-manufactured goods, and continue to U.S. ports. Instead, they must proceed directly to U.S. ports, where distributors <a href="/wiki/Break_bulk_cargo" title="Break bulk cargo">break bulk</a> and send Puerto Rico-bound manufactured goods to Puerto Rico across the ocean by U.S.-flagged ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erg-trade_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-erg-trade-284">[257]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Government of Puerto Rico">local government of Puerto Rico</a> has requested several times to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a> to exclude Puerto Rico from the Jones Act restrictions without success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[ab]</a></sup> The most recent measure has been taken by the <a href="/wiki/17th_Legislative_Assembly_of_Puerto_Rico" title="17th Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico">17th Legislative Assembly of Puerto Rico</a> through R. Conc. del S. 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[260]</a></sup> These measures have always received support from all the <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Political party strength in Puerto Rico">major local political parties</a>.</p>
<p>In 2013 the <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> published a report which concluded that "repealing or amending the Jones Act cabotage law might cut Puerto Rico shipping costs" and that "shippers believed that opening the trade to non-U.S.-flag competition could lower costs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joc-gao-report-repeal-quote_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-joc-gao-report-repeal-quote-290">[ac]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joc-gao-report-lower-costs-quote_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-joc-gao-report-lower-costs-quote-291">[ad]</a></sup> However, the same GAO report also found that "[shippers] doing business in Puerto Rico that GAO contacted reported that the freight rates are often—although not always—lower for foreign carriers going to and from Puerto Rico and foreign locations than the rates shippers pay to ship similar cargo to and from the United States, despite longer distances. Data were not available to allow us to validate the examples given or verify the extent to which this difference occurred."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gao-report-pdf_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-gao-report-pdf-292">[262]</a></sup> Ultimately, the report concluded that "[the] effects of modifying the application of the Jones Act for Puerto Rico are highly uncertain" for both Puerto Rico and the United States, particularly for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Merchant_Marine" title="United States Merchant Marine">U.S. shipping industry</a> and the military preparedness of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joc-gao-report_289-2"><a href="#cite_note-joc-gao-report-289">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gao-report-pdf_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-gao-report-pdf-292">[262]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Demographics of Puerto Rico">Demographics of Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diversity_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Cultural diversity in Puerto Rico">Cultural diversity in Puerto Rico</a></div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_Density,_PR,_2000_(sample).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1204" data-file-width="4000" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg/350px-Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg/525px-Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg/700px-Population_Density%2C_PR%2C_2000_%28sample%29.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_Density,_PR,_2000_(sample).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Population density, Census 2000</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p>The population of Puerto Rico has been shaped by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Amerindian settlement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">European colonization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, economic migration, and Puerto Rico's status as unincorporated territory of the United States.</p>

<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="border-top:1px solid black;font-size:85%;text-align:left">1765–2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[263]</a></sup><br/>
2016 Estimate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopEstUS2016_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstUS2016-4">[4]</a></sup></td>

<p>The estimated population of Puerto Rico as of July 1, 2015, was 3,474,182, a 6.75% decrease since the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopEstUS_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstUS-294">[264]</a></sup> From 2000 to 2010, the population decreased, the first such decrease in census history for Puerto Rico. It went from the 3,808,610 residents registered in the 2000 Census to 3,725,789 in the 2010 Census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall-295">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>A declining and aging population presents additional problems for the society. The US Census Bureau's estimate for July 1, 2016 was 3,411,307 people, down substantially from the 2010 data which had indicated 3,725,789 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-296">[266]</a></sup></p>
<p>Continuous European immigration and high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_increase" title="Natural increase">natural increase</a> helped the population of Puerto Rico grow from 155,426 in 1800, to almost a million by the close of the 19th century.</p>
<p>A census conducted by royal decree on September 30, 1858 gave the following totals of the Puerto Rican population at that time: 341,015 were Free <a href="/wiki/Colored" title="Colored">colored</a>; 300,430 identified as <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">Whites</a>; and 41,736 were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slaves" title="Slaves">slaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[267]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 19th century hundreds of families arrived in Puerto Rico, primarily from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>, but also from other parts of Spain such as <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a> and numerous Spanish loyalists from Spain's former colonies in South America. Settlers from outside Spain also arrived in the islands, including from <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="Chinese immigration to Puerto Rico">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="Irish immigration to Puerto Rico">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="German immigration to Puerto Rico">Germany</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">Italy</a>. This immigration from non-Hispanic countries was the result of the <i>Real Cedula de Gracias de 1815</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Royal_Decree_of_Graces_of_1815" title="Royal Decree of Graces of 1815">Royal Decree of Graces of 1815</a>"), which allowed European Catholics to settle in the island with land allotments in the interior of the island, provided they paid taxes and continued to support the Catholic Church.</p>

<p>Between 1960 and 1990 the census questionnaire in Puerto Rico did not ask about race or ethnicity. The <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">2000 United States Census</a> included a racial self-identification question in Puerto Rico. According to the census, most Puerto Ricans identified as White and Hispanic; few identified as Black or some other race.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_genetics">Population genetics</span></h4>
<p>A recent <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">population genetics</a> study conducted in Puerto Rico suggests that between 52.6% and 84% of the population possess some degree of Amerindian <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) in their maternal ancestry, usually in a combination with other ancestries such as aboriginal <a href="/wiki/Guanches" title="Guanches">Guanche</a> North-West African ancestry brought by Spanish settlers from the Canary Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, these DNA studies show Amerindian ancestry in addition to the Taíno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-native_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-native-300">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madrigal_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madrigal-301">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>One genetic study on the racial makeup of Puerto Ricans (including all races) found them to be roughly around 61% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Eurasian" title="West Eurasian">West Eurasian</a>/<a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> (overwhelmingly of Spanish provenance), 27% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_African" title="Sub-Saharan African">Sub-Saharan African</a> and 11% <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[274]</a></sup> Another genetic study from 2007, claimed that "the average genomewide individual (ie. Puerto Rican) ancestry proportions have been estimated as 66%, 18%, and 16%, for European, West African, and Native American, respectively."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[275]</a></sup> Other study estimates 63.7% European, 21.2% (Sub-Saharan) African, and 15.2% Native American; European ancestry is more prevalent in the West and in Central Puerto Rico, African in Eastern Puerto Rico, and Native American in Northern Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[276]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Pew Research survey indicated a literacy rate of 90.4% (adult population) in 2012 based on data from the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and a life expectancy of 79.3 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-307">[277]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration_and_emigration">Immigration and emigration</span></h3>
