<p>Puerto Rico has recently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> become the permanent home of over 100,000 legal residents. The vast majority of recent immigrants, both legal and illegal, come from the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="Dominican Republic immigration to Puerto Rico">Dominican Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haitian_diaspora" title="Haitian diaspora">Haiti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refworld.org_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-refworld.org-269">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[281]</a></sup> Other sources sending in significant numbers of recent immigrants include <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[283]</a></sup> Also, there are many non-Puerto Rican US citizens settling in Puerto Rico, from the mainland United States and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Virgin_Islands" title="US Virgin Islands">US Virgin Islands</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Nuyorican" title="Nuyorican">Nuyoricans</a> (stateside Puerto Ricans) coming back to Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[284]</a></sup> Most recent immigrants settle in and around San Juan.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">Emigration</a> is a major part of contemporary Puerto Rican history. Starting soon after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, poverty, cheap airfares, and promotion by the island government caused waves of Puerto Ricans to move to the United States, particularly to the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern states</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[285]</a></sup> This trend continued even as Puerto Rico's economy improved and its birth rate declined. Puerto Ricans continue to follow a pattern of "<a href="/wiki/Circular_migration" title="Circular migration">circular migration</a>", with some migrants returning to the island. In recent years, the population has declined markedly, falling nearly 1% in 2012 and an additional 1% (36,000 people) in 2013 due to a falling birthrate and emigration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the 2010 Census, the number of Puerto Ricans living in the United States outside of Puerto Rico far exceeds those living in Puerto Rico. Emigration exceeds immigeation. As those who leave tend to be better educated than those that remain, this accentuates the drain on Puerto Rico’s economy.</p>
<p>Based on the July 1, 2016 estimate by the US Census Bureau, the population of the Commonwealth had declined by 314,482 people since the 2010 Census data had been tabulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-296">[266]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_distribution">Population distribution</span></h3>
<p>The most populous city is the capital, <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a>, with approximately 371,400 people based on a 2015 estimate by the Census Bureau.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[287]</a></sup> Other major cities include <a href="/wiki/Bayam%C3%B3n,_Puerto_Rico" title="Bayamón, Puerto Rico">Bayamón</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carolina,_Puerto_Rico" title="Carolina, Puerto Rico">Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caguas,_Puerto_Rico" title="Caguas, Puerto Rico">Caguas</a>. Of the ten most populous cities on the island, eight are located within what is considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Juan-Caguas-Guaynabo_metropolitan_area" title="San Juan-Caguas-Guaynabo metropolitan area">San Juan's metropolitan area</a>, while the other two are located in the south (<a href="/wiki/Ponce_metropolitan_area" title="Ponce metropolitan area">Ponce</a>) and west (<a href="/wiki/Mayag%C3%BCez_metropolitan_area" title="Mayagüez metropolitan area">Mayagüez</a>) of the island.</p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[289]</a></sup> of the executive branch of government of Puerto Rico<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[290]</a></sup> are Spanish and English, with Spanish being the primary language. Spanish is, and has been, the only official language of the entire Commonwealth judiciary system, despite a 1902 English-only language law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[291]</a></sup> All official business of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._District_Court" title="U.S. District Court">U.S. District Court</a> for the District of Puerto Rico is conducted in English. English is the primary language of less than 10% of the population. Spanish is the dominant language of business, education and daily life on the island, spoken by nearly 95% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factfinder.census.gov_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder.census.gov-322">[292]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US Census Bureau's 2015 update provides the following facts:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[293]</a></sup> 94.1% of adults speak Spanish, 5.8% speak only English, 78.3% do not speak English "very well".</p>
<p>In Puerto Rico, public school instruction is conducted almost entirely in Spanish. There have been pilot programs in about a dozen of the over 1,400 public schools aimed at conducting instruction in English only. Objections from teaching staff are common, perhaps because many of them are not fully fluent in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[294]</a></sup> English is taught as a second language and is a compulsory subject from elementary levels to high school. The languages of the deaf community are <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">American Sign Language</a> and its local variant, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Sign_Language" title="Puerto Rican Sign Language">Puerto Rican Sign Language</a>.</p>
<p>The Spanish of Puerto Rico has evolved into having many idiosyncrasies in vocabulary and syntax that differentiate it from the Spanish spoken elsewhere. As a product of Puerto Rican history, the island possesses a unique Spanish dialect. Puerto Rican Spanish utilizes many Taíno words, as well as English words. The largest influence on the Spanish spoken in Puerto Rico is that of the Canary Islands. The Spanish of Puerto Rico also includes <a href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno" title="Taíno">Taíno</a> words, typically in the context of vegetation, natural phenomena or primitive musical instruments. Similarly, words attributed to primarily West <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">African languages</a> were adopted in the contexts of foods, music or dances, particularly in coastal towns with concentrations of descendants of Sub-Saharan Africans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[295]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">
<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden">
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:Black"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 181.89932472811px; border-left-color:Red"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Red"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Red"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 120.87923504096px; border-left-color:Violet"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Violet"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:Violet"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:100px; border-width:36.812455268468px 92.977648588825px 0 0; border-top-color:Blue"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:Blue"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:0;top:0"><img alt="Circle frame.svg" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/200px-Circle_frame.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/300px-Circle_frame.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Circle_frame.svg/400px-Circle_frame.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Religious Affiliation (2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[296]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[297]</a></sup></p>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Blue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> (56%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Violet; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> (33%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Other (3%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irreligious" title="Irreligious">Irreligious</a> (8%)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Roman Catholicism in Puerto Rico">Roman Catholic Church</a> was brought by Spanish colonists and gradually became the dominant religion in Puerto Rico. The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioceses" title="Dioceses">dioceses</a> in the Americas, including that of Puerto Rico, were authorized by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Julius_II" title="Pope Julius II">Pope Julius II</a> in 1511.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[298]</a></sup> One Pope, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Paul_II" title="John Paul II">John Paul II</a>, visited Puerto Rico in October 1984. All <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Municipalities of Puerto Rico">municipalities</a> in Puerto Rico have at least one Catholic Church, most of which are located at the town center or "<i>plaza</i>". African slaves brought and maintained various ethnic African religious practices associated with different peoples; in particular, the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba</a> beliefs of <a href="/wiki/Santer%C3%ADa" title="Santería">Santería</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/If%C3%A1" title="Ifá">Ifá</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">Kongo</a>-derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palo_Mayombe" title="Palo Mayombe">Palo Mayombe</a>. Some aspects were absorbed into syncretic Christianity.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Protestantism in Puerto Rico">Protestantism</a>, which was suppressed under the Spanish Catholic regime, has slightly reemerged under United States rule, making contemporary Puerto Rico more interconfessional than in previous centuries, although Catholicism continues to be the dominant religion. The first Protestant church, <a href="/wiki/Iglesia_de_la_Sant%C3%ADsima_Trinidad" title="Iglesia de la Santísima Trinidad">Iglesia de la Santísima Trinidad</a>, was established in Ponce by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Antigua" title="Diocese of Antigua">Diocese of Antigua</a> in 1872.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[299]</a></sup> It was the first non-Roman Catholic Church in the entire <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Americas" title="The Americas">the Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[301]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pollster Pablo Ramos stated in 1998 that the population was 38% Roman Catholic, 28% Pentecostal, and 18% were members of independent churches, which would give a Protestant percentage of 46% if the last two populations are combined. Protestants collectively added up to almost two million people. Another researcher gave a more conservative assessment of the proportion of Protestants:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Puerto Rico, by virtue of its long political association with the United States, is the most Protestant of Latin American countries, with a Protestant population of approximately 33 to 38 percent, the majority of whom are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostal" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a>. David Stoll calculates that if we extrapolate the growth rates of evangelical churches from 1960–1985 for another twenty-five years Puerto Rico will become 75 percent evangelical. (Ana Adams: "Brincando el Charco..." in <i>Power, Politics and Pentecostals in Latin America</i>, Edward Cleary, ed., 1997. p. 164).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[302]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The data provided for 2014 by <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-307">[277]</a></sup> is summarized in the chart to the right. An <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> article in March 2014 stated that "more than 70 percent of whom identify themselves as Catholic" but provided no source for this information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[303]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CIA World Factbook reports that 85% of the population of Puerto Rico identifies as Roman Catholic, while 15% identify as Protestant and Other. Neither a date or a source for that information is provided and may not be recent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[304]</a></sup> A 2013 Pew Research survey found that only about 45% of Puerto Rican adults identified themselves as Catholic, 29% as Protestant and 20% as unaffiliated with a religion. The people surveyed by Pew consisted of Puerto Ricans living in the 50 states and DC and may not be indicative of those living in the Commonwealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[305]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 2014, a Pew Research report, with the sub-title <i>Widespread Change in a Historically Catholic Region</i>, indicated that only 56% of Puerto Ricans were Catholic and that 33% were Protestant; this survey was completed between October 2013 and February 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[306]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> community, the Dormition of the Most Holy Theotokos / St. Spyridon's Church is located in Trujillo Alto, and serves the small Orthodox community. This affiliation accounted for under 1% of the population in 2010 according to the Pew Research report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[307]</a></sup> In 1940, <a href="/wiki/Juanita_Garc%C3%ADa_Peraza" title="Juanita García Peraza">Juanita García Peraza</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Mita_Congregation" title="Mita Congregation">Mita Congregation</a>, the first religion of Puerto Rican origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[308]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" title="Taíno people">Taíno religious practices</a> have been rediscovered/reinvented to a degree by a handful of advocates. Similarly, some aspects of African religious traditions have been kept by some adherents. In 1952, a handful of American Jews established the island's first <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a>; this religion accounts for under 1% of the population in 2010 according to the Pew Research report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EG_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-EG-339">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[310]</a></sup> The synagogue, called <i>Sha'are Zedeck</i>, hired its first rabbi in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JVL_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-341">[311]</a></sup> Puerto Rico has the largest Jewish community in the Caribbean, numbering 3000 people (date not stated),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[312]</a></sup> and is the only Caribbean island in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_Jews" title="Conservative Jews">Conservative</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reform_Jews" title="Reform Jews">Reform</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jews" title="Orthodox Jews">Orthodox Jewish</a> movements all are represented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JVL_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-341">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LN_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-LN-343">[313]</a></sup> In 2007, there were about 5,000 Muslims in Puerto Rico, representing about 0.13% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[315]</a></sup> Eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosques" title="Mosques">mosques</a> are located throughout the island, with most Muslims living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Piedras" title="Río Piedras">Río Piedras</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caguas" title="Caguas">Caguas</a>, most of these Muslims are of Palestinian and Jordanian descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[317]</a></sup> In 2015, the 25,832 <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> represented about 0.70% of the population, with 324 congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[318]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico" title="Chinese immigration to Puerto Rico">Padmasambhava Buddhist Center</a>, whose followers practice <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>, has a branch in Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[319]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Education in Puerto Rico">Education in Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The first school in Puerto Rico was the <i>Escuela de Gramática</i> (Grammar School). It was established by Bishop <a href="/wiki/Alonso_Manso" title="Alonso Manso">Alonso Manso</a> in 1513, in the area where the Cathedral of San Juan was to be constructed. The school was free of charge and the courses taught were Latin language, literature, history, science, art, philosophy and theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[320]</a></sup></p>
<p>Education in Puerto Rico is divided in three levels—Primary (elementary school grades 1–6), Secondary (intermediate and high school grades 7–12), and Higher Level (undergraduate and graduate studies). As of 2002, the literacy rate of the Puerto Rican population was 94.1%; by gender, it was 93.9% for males and 94.4% for females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[321]</a></sup> According to the 2000 Census, 60.0% of the population attained a high school degree or higher level of education, and 18.3% has a bachelor's degree or higher.</p>
<p>Instruction at the primary school level is compulsory and enforced by the state between the ages of 5 and 18. The Constitution of Puerto Rico grants the right to an education to every citizen on the island. To this end, public schools in Puerto Rico provide free and non-sectarian education at the elementary, and secondary levels. At any of the three levels, students may attend either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" title="Public school (government funded)">public</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_School" title="Private School">private</a> schools. By 1999, there were 1532 public schools<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[322]</a></sup> and 569 private schools in the island. As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 1539 public schools and 806 private schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[323]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest and oldest university system is the public <a href="/wiki/University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="University of Puerto Rico">University of Puerto Rico</a> (UPR) with 11 campuses. The largest private university systems on the island are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sistema_Universitario_Ana_G._Mendez" title="Sistema Universitario Ana G. Mendez">Sistema Universitario Ana G. Mendez</a> which operates the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Turabo" title="University of Turabo">Universidad del Turabo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_University_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Metropolitan University (Puerto Rico)">Metropolitan University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universidad_del_Este" title="Universidad del Este">Universidad del Este</a>. Other private universities include the multi-campus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inter_American_University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Inter American University of Puerto Rico">Inter American University</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Catholic University of Puerto Rico">Pontifical Catholic University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polytechnic_University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Polytechnic University of Puerto Rico">Universidad Politécnica de Puerto Rico</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_the_Sacred_Heart_(Puerto_Rico)" title="University of the Sacred Heart (Puerto Rico)">Universidad del Sagrado Corazón</a>. Puerto Rico has four schools of Medicine and three ABA-approved Law Schools.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> medical care in Puerto Rico had been heavily impacted by emigration of doctors to the mainland and underfunding of the Medicare and Medicaid programs which serve 60% of the island's population. Affordable medical insurance under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affordable_Care_Act" title="Affordable Care Act">Affordable Care Act</a> is not available in Puerto Rico as, since Puerto Ricans pay no income tax, no subsidies are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT8215_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT8215-354">[324]</a></sup></p>
<p>The city of <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a> has a system of <a href="/wiki/Triage" title="Triage">triage</a>, hospital, and preventive care health services. The municipal government sponsors regular health fairs in different areas of the city focusing on health care for the elderly and the disabled.</p>
<p>There are twenty hospitals in San Juan, half of which are operated by the government. The largest hospital is the <i>Centro Médico de Río Piedras</i> (the Río Piedras Medical Center). Founded in 1956, it is operated by the Medical Services Administration of the Department of Health of Puerto Rico, and is actually a network of eight hospitals:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>San Juan Municipal Hospital: This hospital is operated by the San Juan municipal government.</li>
<li>Industrial Hospital: This is the hospital for Puerto Rico government employees, whether municipal or Commonwealth government employees. Normally, injured police officers and firefighters are cared for here.</li>
<li>San Juan Pediatric Hospital – Also operated by the San Juan municipal government.</li>
<li>Pediatric Hospital: Operated by the government of the Commonwealth, this is the main trauma hospital for pediatric cases.</li>
<li>Centro Medico Emergency Room: This is the main hospital for trauma cases for Puerto Rico and the Caribbean.</li>
<li><i>Centro Cardiovascular del Caribe</i> (Caribbean Cardiovascular Center): This is the main hospital for open heart surgery in the Caribbean. It features a hotel for the patients' families.</li>
<li>Psychiatric Hospital: The main psychiatric hospital in Puerto Rico. Operated by the government of Puerto Rico.</li>
<li>Psychiatric Correctional Hospital: It is both a hospital and correctional facility. It is operated jointly by the Puerto Rico Department of Corrections and the Medical Services Administration.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>The city of San Juan operates nine other hospitals. Of these, eight are Diagnostic and Treatment Centers located in communities throughout San Juan. These nine hospitals are:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/La_Perla,_San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="La Perla, San Juan, Puerto Rico">La Perla</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Puerta_de_Tierra,_Puerto_Rico" title="Puerta de Tierra, Puerto Rico">Puerta de Tierra</a></li>
<li>Llorens Torres</li>
<li>Puerto Nuevo</li>
<li>San José</li>
<li>Río Piedras</li>
<li>Sabana Llana</li>
<li>Hoare</li>
<li>Santurce Parada 19</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>There are also ten private hospitals in San Juan. These are:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li><i>Hospital Metropilitano</i></li>
<li><i>Hospital Auxilio Mutuo</i></li>
<li><i>Hospital Auxilio Mutuo Expreso</i></li>
<li>Hospital de Veteranos: The main Veterans hospital in the Caribbean. Operated by the U.S. Veteran Healthcare System.</li>
<li>Ashford Presbyterian Hospital</li>
<li>Hospital Pavia Hato Rey</li>
<li>Hospital Pavia Santurce</li>
<li>San Jorge Children's Hospital: The most well known children's hospital in the San Juan Metropolitan Area.</li>
<li>Hospital San Gerardo: Located at the Cupey neighborhood, is a small hospital but is also specialized in psychiatry and elderly.</li>
<li>Hospital del Maestro (Teachers Hospital): Located in Hato Rey, this hospital is operated by the Puerto Rico Teachers Association.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce</a> is served by several clinics and hospitals. There are four comprehensive care hospitals: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospital_Dr._Pila" title="Hospital Dr. Pila">Hospital Dr. Pila</a>, Hospital San Cristobal, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospital_San_Lucas" title="Hospital San Lucas">Hospital San Lucas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[325]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospital_de_Damas" title="Hospital de Damas">Hospital de Damas</a>. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Hospital_Oncol%C3%B3gico_Andr%C3%A9s_Grillasca" title="Hospital Oncológico Andrés Grillasca">Hospital Oncológico Andrés Grillasca</a> specializes in the treatment of cancer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[326]</a></sup> and Hospital Siquiátrico specializes in mental disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[327]</a></sup> There is also a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">U.S. Department of Veterans Affairs</a> Outpatient Clinic that provides health services to U.S. veterans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[328]</a></sup> The U.S. Veterans Administration will build a new hospital in the city to satisfy regional needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[329]</a></sup> Hospital de Damas is listed in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a> as one of the best hospitals under the U.S. flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[330]</a></sup> Ponce has the highest concentration of medical infrastructure per inhabitant of any municipality in Puerto Rico.</p>
<p>On the island of <a href="/wiki/Culebra,_Puerto_Rico" title="Culebra, Puerto Rico">Culebra</a>, there is a small hospital in the island called <i>Hospital de Culebra</i>. It also offers <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy" title="Pharmacy">pharmacy</a> services to residents and visitors. For emergencies, patients are transported by plane to <a href="/wiki/Fajardo,_Puerto_Rico" title="Fajardo, Puerto Rico">Fajardo</a> on the main island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[331]</a></sup></p>
<p>The town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caguas" title="Caguas">Caguas</a> has three hospitals: Hospital Hima San Pablo, Menonita Caguas Regional Hospital, and the San Juan Bautista Medical Center.</p>
<p>The town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cayey" title="Cayey">Cayey</a> is served by the <i>Hospital Menonita de Cayey</i>, and the <i>Hospital Municipal de Cayey.</i></p>
<p><i>Reforma de Salud de Puerto Rico</i> (<a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Health_Reform" title="Puerto Rico Health Reform">Puerto Rico Health Reform</a>) – locally referred to as <i>La Reforma</i> (The Reform) – is a government-run program which provides medical and health care services to the indigent and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impoverished" title="Impoverished">impoverished</a>, by means of contracting private <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> companies, rather than employing government-owned hospitals and emergency centers. The Reform is administered by the Puerto Rico Health Insurance Administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Form_10K_362-0"><a href="#cite_note-Form_10K-362">[332]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Culture of Puerto Rico">Culture of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>Modern Puerto Rican culture is a unique mix of cultural antecedents: including people European (predominantly Spanish, Italian, French, German and Irish), African, and, more recently, some North American and lots of South Americans. A large number of Cubans and Dominican have relocated to the island in the past few decades.</p>
<p>From the Spanish, Puerto Rico received the Spanish language, the Catholic religion and the vast majority of their cultural and moral values and traditions. The United States added English-language influence, the university system and the adoption of some holidays and practices. On March 12, 1903, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="University of Puerto Rico">University of Puerto Rico</a> was officially founded, branching out from the "Escuela Normal Industrial", a smaller organization that was founded in Fajardo three years before.</p>
<p>Much of Puerto Rican culture centers on the influence of music and has been shaped by other cultures combining with local and traditional rhythms. Early in the history of Puerto Rican music, the influences of Spanish and African traditions were most noticeable. The cultural movements across the Caribbean and North America have played a vital role in the more recent musical influences which have reached Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[334]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official symbols of Puerto Rico are the <i>reinita mora</i> or <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_spindalis" title="Puerto Rican spindalis">Puerto Rican spindalis</a> (a type of bird), the <a href="/wiki/Thespesia_grandiflora" title="Thespesia grandiflora"><i>flor de maga</i></a> (a type of flower), and the <i>ceiba</i> or <a href="/wiki/Ceiba_pentandra" title="Ceiba pentandra">kapok</a> (a type of tree). The unofficial animal and a symbol of Puerto Rican pride is the <a href="/wiki/Common_coqu%C3%AD" title="Common coquí">coquí</a>, a small frog. Other popular symbols of Puerto Rico are the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C3%ADbaro" title="Jíbaro">jíbaro</a></i> (the "countryman"), and the carite.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Architecture of Puerto Rico">Architecture of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The architecture of Puerto Rico demonstrates a broad variety of traditions, styles and national influences accumulated over four centuries of Spanish rule, and a century of American rule. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_colonial_architecture" title="Spanish colonial architecture">Spanish colonial architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Ibero-Islamic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_deco" title="Art deco">art deco</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-modern_architecture" title="Post-modern architecture">post-modern</a>, and many other architectural forms are visible throughout the island. From town to town, there are also many regional distinctions.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_(28488284470).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg/300px-The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg/450px-The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg/600px-The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_%2828488284470%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Colors_of_Old_San_Juan_(28488284470).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Old_San_Juan" title="Old San Juan">Old San Juan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Old San Juan is one of the two <i>barrios</i>, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Santurce,_San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="Santurce, San Juan, Puerto Rico">Santurce</a>, that made up the <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">municipality</a> of San Juan from 1864 to 1951, at which time the former independent municipality of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%ADo_Piedras" title="Río Piedras">Río Piedras</a> was annexed. With its abundance of shops, historic places, museums, open air cafés, restaurants, gracious homes, tree-shaded plazas, and its old beauty and architectonical peculiarity, Old San Juan is a main spot for local and internal tourism. The district is also characterized by numerous public plazas and churches including <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9_Church" title="San José Church">San José Church</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_San_Juan_Bautista" title="Cathedral of San Juan Bautista">Cathedral of San Juan Bautista</a>, which contains the tomb of the Spanish explorer <a href="/wiki/Juan_Ponce_de_Le%C3%B3n" title="Juan Ponce de León">Juan Ponce de León</a>. It also houses the oldest Catholic school for elementary education in Puerto Rico, the Colegio de Párvulos, built in 1865.</p>
<p>The oldest parts of the district of Old San Juan remain partly enclosed by massive walls. Several defensive structures and notable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort" title="Fort">forts</a>, such as the emblematic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_San_Felipe_del_Morro" title="Fort San Felipe del Morro">Fort San Felipe del Morro</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_San_Crist%C3%B3bal" title="Fort San Cristóbal">Fort San Cristóbal</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Palacio_de_Santa_Catalina" title="El Palacio de Santa Catalina">El Palacio de Santa Catalina</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/La_Fortaleza" title="La Fortaleza">La Fortaleza</a>, acted as the primary defenses of the settlement which was subjected to numerous attacks. <a href="/wiki/La_Fortaleza" title="La Fortaleza">La Fortaleza</a> continues to serve also as the executive mansion for the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Governor of Puerto Rico">Governor of Puerto Rico</a>. Many of the historic fortifications are part of <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_National_Historic_Site" title="San Juan National Historic Site">San Juan National Historic Site</a>.</p>
<p>During the 1940s, sections of Old San Juan fell into disrepair, and many renovation plans were suggested. There was even a strong push to develop Old San Juan as a "small <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>". Strict remodeling codes were implemented to prevent new constructions from affecting the common colonial Spanish architectural themes of the old city. When a project proposal suggested that the old Carmelite Convent in San Juan be demolished to erect a new hotel, the Institute had the building declared as a historic building, and then asked that it be converted to a hotel in a renewed facility. This was what became the <i>Hotel El Convento</i> in Old San Juan. The paradigm to reconstruct and renovate the old city and revitalize it has been followed by other cities in the Americas, particularly <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartagena_de_Indias" title="Cartagena de Indias">Cartagena de Indias</a>.</p>
<p>Ponce Creole is an <a href="/wiki/Architectural_style" title="Architectural style">architectural style</a> created in <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce, Puerto Rico</a>, in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. This style of Puerto Rican buildings is found predominantly in residential homes in <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce</a> that developed between 1895 and 1920. Ponce Creole architecture borrows heavily from the traditions of the French, the Spaniards, and the Caribbean to create houses that were especially built to withstand the hot and dry climate of the region, and to take advantage of the sun and sea breezes characteristic of the southern Puerto Rico's <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[335]</a></sup> It is a blend of wood and masonry, incorporating architectural elements of other styles, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_revival" title="Classical revival">Classical revival</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Revival" title="Spanish Revival">Spanish Revival</a> to <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[336]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_art" title="Puerto Rican art">Puerto Rican art</a></div>
<p>Puerto Rican art reflects many influences, much from its ethnically diverse background. A form of <a href="/wiki/Folk_art" title="Folk art">folk art</a>, called <i>santos</i> evolved from the Catholic Church's use of <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculptures</a> to convert indigenous Puerto Ricans to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. <i>Santos</i> depict figures of saints and other religious icons and are made from native wood, clay, and stone. After shaping simple, they are often finished by painting them in vivid colors. <i>Santos</i> vary in size, with the smallest examples around eight inches tall and the largest about twenty inches tall. Traditionally, santos were seen as messengers between the earth and Heaven. As such, they occupied a special place on household <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altars</a>, where people prayed to them, asked for help, or tried to summon their protection.</p>
<p>Also popular, <i>caretas</i> or <i>vejigantes</i> are masks worn during <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">carnivals</a>. Similar masks signifying evil spirits were used in both Spain and Africa, though for different purposes. The Spanish used their masks to frighten lapsed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> into returning to the church, while tribal Africans used them as protection from the evil spirits they represented. True to their historic origins Puerto Rican <i>caretas</i> always bear at least several horns and fangs. While usually constructed of <a href="/wiki/Papier-m%C3%A2ch%C3%A9" title="Papier-mâché">papier-mâché</a>, coconut shells and fine metal screening are sometimes used as well. Red and black were the typical colors for <i>caretas</i> but their palette has expanded to include a wide variety of bright hues and patterns.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_literature" title="Puerto Rican literature">Puerto Rican literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retrato_de_EMdeHostos_por_Francisco_Oller.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="249" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Retrato_de_EMdeHostos_por_Francisco_Oller.jpg/170px-Retrato_de_EMdeHostos_por_Francisco_Oller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Retrato_de_EMdeHostos_por_Francisco_Oller.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retrato_de_EMdeHostos_por_Francisco_Oller.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eugenio_Mar%C3%ADa_de_Hostos" title="Eugenio María de Hostos">Eugenio María de Hostos</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Puerto Rican literature evolved from the art of <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">oral story telling</a> to its present-day status. Written works by the native islanders of Puerto Rico were prohibited and repressed by the Spanish colonial government. Only those who were commissioned by the Spanish Crown to document the chronological history of the island were allowed to write.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diego_de_Torres_Vargas" title="Diego de Torres Vargas">Diego de Torres Vargas</a> was allowed to circumvent this strict prohibition for three reasons: he was a priest, he came from a prosperous Spanish family, and his father was a Sergeant Major in the Spanish Army, who died while defending Puerto Rico from an invasion by the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> armada. In 1647, Torres Vargas wrote <i>Descripción de la Ciudad e Isla de Puerto Rico</i> ("Description of the Island and City of Puerto Rico"). This historical book was the first to make a detailed geographic description of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DT_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-DT-367">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>The book described all the fruits and commercial establishments of the time, mostly centered in the towns of San Juan and Ponce. The book also listed and described every mine, church, and hospital in the island at the time. The book contained notices on the State and Capital, plus an extensive and erudite bibliography. <i>Descripción de la Ciudad e Isla de Puerto Rico</i> was the first successful attempt at writing a comprehensive history of Puerto Rico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DT_367-1"><a href="#cite_note-DT-367">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of Puerto Rico's earliest writers were influenced by the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Cordero_(educator)" title="Rafael Cordero (educator)">Rafael Cordero</a>. Among these was Dr. <a href="/wiki/Manuel_A._Alonso" title="Manuel A. Alonso">Manuel A. Alonso</a>, the first Puerto Rican writer of notable importance. In 1849 he published <i>El Gíbaro</i>, a collection of verses whose main themes were the poor Puerto Rican country farmer. <a href="/wiki/Eugenio_Mar%C3%ADa_de_Hostos" title="Eugenio María de Hostos">Eugenio María de Hostos</a> wrote <i>La peregrinación de Bayoán</i> in 1863, which used Bartolomé de las Casas as a spring board to reflect on Caribbean identity. After this first novel, Hostos abandoned fiction in favor of the essay which he saw as offering greater possibilities for inspiring social change.</p>
<p>In the late 19th century, with the arrival of the first printing press and the founding of the Royal Academy of Belles Letters, Puerto Rican literature began to flourish. The first writers to express their political views in regard to Spanish colonial rule of the island were journalists. After the United States invaded Puerto Rico during the Spanish–American War and the island was ceded to the Americans as a condition of the Treaty of Paris of 1898, writers and poets began to express their opposition to the new colonial rule by writing about patriotic themes.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Tapia_y_Rivera" title="Alejandro Tapia y Rivera">Alejandro Tapia y Rivera</a>, also known as the Father of Puerto Rican Literature, ushered in a new age of <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a> with the publication of <i>The Historical Library of Puerto Rico</i>. <a href="/wiki/Cayetano_Coll_y_Toste" title="Cayetano Coll y Toste">Cayetano Coll y Toste</a> was another Puerto Rican historian and writer. His work <i>The Indo-Antillano Vocabulary</i> is valuable in understanding the way the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADnos" title="Taínos">Taínos</a> lived. Dr. <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Zeno_Gand%C3%ADa" title="Manuel Zeno Gandía">Manuel Zeno Gandía</a> in 1894 wrote <i>La Charca</i> and told about the harsh life in the remote and mountainous coffee regions in Puerto Rico. Dr. <a href="/wiki/Antonio_S._Pedreira" title="Antonio S. Pedreira">Antonio S. Pedreira</a>, described in his work <i>Insularismo</i> the cultural survival of the Puerto Rican identity after the American invasion.</p>
<p>With the Puerto Rican diaspora of the 1940s, Puerto Rican literature was greatly influenced by a phenomenon known as the <a href="/wiki/Nuyorican_Movement" title="Nuyorican Movement">Nuyorican Movement</a>. Puerto Rican literature continued to flourish and many Puerto Ricans have since distinguished themselves as authors, journalists, poets, novelists, playwrights, screenwriters, essayists and have also stood out in other literary fields. The influence of Puerto Rican literature has transcended the boundaries of the island to the United States and the rest of the world. Over the past fifty years, significant writers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ed_Vega" title="Ed Vega">Ed Vega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Rafael_S%C3%A1nchez" title="Luis Rafael Sánchez">Luis Rafael Sánchez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piri_Thomas" title="Piri Thomas">Piri Thomas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giannina_Braschi" title="Giannina Braschi">Giannina Braschi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Pi%C3%B1ero" title="Miguel Piñero">Miguel Piñero</a>. <a href="/wiki/Esmeralda_Santiago" title="Esmeralda Santiago">Esmeralda Santiago</a> has written an autobiographical trilogy about growing up in modern Puerto Rico as well as an historical novel, <i>Conquistadora</i>, about life on a sugar plantation during the mid-19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_in_Puerto_Rico" title="Media in Puerto Rico">Media in Puerto Rico</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">media</a> in Puerto Rico includes local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_stations" title="Radio stations">radio stations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_stations" title="Television stations">television stations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newspapers" title="Newspapers">newspapers</a>, the majority of which are conducted in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>. There are also three stations of the <a href="/wiki/American_Forces_Network" title="American Forces Network">U.S. Armed Forces Radio and Television Service</a>. Newspapers with daily distribution are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Nuevo_Dia" title="El Nuevo Dia">El Nuevo Dia</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Vocero" title="El Vocero">El Vocero</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Indice_(newspaper)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Indice (newspaper) (page does not exist)">Indice</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Metro_(Puerto_Rico)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Metro (Puerto Rico) (page does not exist)">Metro</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Primera_Hora_(Puerto_Rico)" title="Primera Hora (Puerto Rico)">Primera Hora</a>. <a href="/wiki/El_Vocero" title="El Vocero">El Vocero</a> is distributed free of charge as well as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Indice_(newspaper)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Indice (newspaper) (page does not exist)">Indice</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Metro_(Puerto_Rico)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Metro (Puerto Rico) (page does not exist)">Metro</a>.</p>
<p>Newspapers distributed on a weekly or regional basis include <a href="/wiki/Claridad" title="Claridad">Claridad</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Perla_del_Sur" title="La Perla del Sur">La Perla del Sur</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Opinion" title="La Opinion">La Opinion</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vision_(Puerto_Rico)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vision (Puerto Rico) (page does not exist)">Vision</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=La_Estrella_del_Norte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="La Estrella del Norte (page does not exist)">La Estrella del Norte</a>, among others. Several television channels provide local content in the island. These include <a href="/wiki/WIPR-TV" title="WIPR-TV">WIPR-TV</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telemundo_Puerto_Rico_(TV_channel)" title="Telemundo Puerto Rico (TV channel)">Telemundo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Univision_Puerto_Rico" title="Univision Puerto Rico">Univision Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/WAPA-TV" title="WAPA-TV">WAPA-TV</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WKAQ-TV" title="WKAQ-TV">WKAQ-TV</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Music of Puerto Rico">Music of Puerto Rico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
